title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="\'h\' in \'hi\'">h</span><span title="/&#593;&#720;/: \'a\' in \'father\'">&#593;&#720;</span><span title="\'n\' in \'nigh\'">n</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%BC%A2%E6%9C%9D" title="wikt:&#28450;&#26397;">&#28450;&#26397;</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">H&#224;nch&#225;o</span></i>) was the second <a href="/wiki/Dynasties_in_Chinese_history" title="Dynasties in Chinese history">imperial dynasty of China</a> (206 BC&#8211;220 AD), preceded by the <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin dynasty</a> (221&#8211;206 BC) and succeeded by the <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms" title="Three Kingdoms">Three Kingdoms</a> period (220&#8211;280 AD). Spanning over four centuries, the Han period is considered a <a href="/wiki/Golden_age_(metaphor)" title="Golden age (metaphor)">golden age</a> in Chinese history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZhou200334_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZhou200334-5">[5]</a></sup> To this day, China\'s <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">majority ethnic group</a> refers to themselves as the "Han Chinese" and the Chinese script is referred to as "<a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Han characters</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchaefer2008279_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaefer2008279-6">[6]</a></sup> It was founded by the rebel leader <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liu_Bang" title="Liu Bang">Liu Bang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Posthumous_name" title="Posthumous name">known posthumously</a> as <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Gaozu_of_Han" title="Emperor Gaozu of Han">Emperor Gaozu of Han</a>, and briefly interrupted by the <a href="/wiki/Xin_dynasty" title="Xin dynasty">Xin dynasty</a> (9&#8211;23 AD) of the former regent <a href="/wiki/Wang_Mang" title="Wang Mang">Wang Mang</a>. This interregnum separates the Han dynasty into two periods: the <a href="#Western_Han">Western Han</a> or Former Han (206 BC&#160;&#8211; 9 AD) and the <a href="#Eastern_Han">Eastern Han</a> or Later Han (25&#8211;220&#160;AD).\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_China" title="Emperor of China">The emperor</a> was at the pinnacle of <a href="/wiki/Society_and_culture_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="Society and culture of the Han dynasty">Han society</a>. He presided over the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="Government of the Han dynasty">Han government</a> but shared power with both <a href="/wiki/Chinese_nobility" title="Chinese nobility">the nobility</a> and appointed ministers who came largely from the scholarly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gentry_(China)" title="Gentry (China)">gentry class</a>. The Han Empire was divided into areas directly controlled by the central government using an innovation inherited from the Qin known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commandery_(China)" title="Commandery (China)">commanderies</a>, and a number of <a href="/wiki/Kings_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="Kings of the Han dynasty">semi-autonomous kingdoms</a>. These kingdoms gradually lost all vestiges of their independence, particularly following the <a href="/wiki/Rebellion_of_the_Seven_States" title="Rebellion of the Seven States">Rebellion of the Seven States</a>. From the reign of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wu_of_Han" title="Emperor Wu of Han">Emperor Wu</a> (r.&#160;141&#8211;87 BC) onward, the Chinese court officially sponsored <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a> in education and court politics, synthesized with the <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmology</a> of later scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Dong_Zhongshu" title="Dong Zhongshu">Dong Zhongshu</a>. This policy endured until the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a> in 1911 AD.\n</p><p>The Han dynasty saw an <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="Economy of the Han dynasty">age of economic prosperity</a> and witnessed a significant growth of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_currency#Imperial_China" title="Chinese currency">money economy</a> first established during the <a href="/wiki/Zhou_dynasty" title="Zhou dynasty">Zhou dynasty</a> (c.&#160;1050&#8211;256 BC). <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Chinese_coinage" title="Ancient Chinese coinage">The coinage</a> issued by the central government <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mint_(coin)" title="Mint (coin)">mint</a> in 119 BC remained the standard coinage of China until the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a> (618&#8211;907 AD). The period saw a number of limited institutional innovations. To <a href="/wiki/Army_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="Army of the Han dynasty">finance its military campaigns</a> and the settlement of newly conquered frontier territories, the Han government <a href="/wiki/Nationalization" title="Nationalization">nationalized</a> the private salt and iron industries in 117 BC, but these government monopolies were repealed during the Eastern Han dynasty. <a href="/wiki/Science_and_technology_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="Science and technology of the Han dynasty">Science and technology during the Han period</a> saw significant advances, including the process of <a href="/wiki/Papermaking" title="Papermaking">papermaking</a>, the nautical steering ship <a href="/wiki/Rudder" title="Rudder">rudder</a>, the use of <a href="/wiki/Negative_number" title="Negative number">negative numbers</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_mathematics" title="Chinese mathematics">mathematics</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Raised-relief_map" title="Raised-relief map">raised-relief map</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hydraulics" title="Hydraulics">hydraulic</a>-powered <a href="/wiki/Armillary_sphere" title="Armillary sphere">armillary sphere</a> for <a href="/wiki/Chinese_astronomy" title="Chinese astronomy">astronomy</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Seismometer" title="Seismometer">seismometer</a> employing an <a href="/wiki/Inverted_pendulum" title="Inverted pendulum">inverted pendulum</a> that could be used to discern the cardinal direction of distant earthquakes.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Xiongnu" title="Xiongnu">Xiongnu</a>, a nomadic <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Steppe" title="Eurasian Steppe">steppe</a> confederation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBailey198525&#8211;26_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBailey198525&#8211;26-7">[7]</a></sup> defeated the Han in 200&#160;BC and forced the Han to submit as a <i>de facto</i> inferior and vassal partner, but continued their military raids on the Han borders. Emperor Wu launched <a href="/wiki/Han%E2%80%93Xiongnu_War" title="Han&#8211;Xiongnu War">several military campaigns</a> against them. The ultimate Han victory in these wars eventually forced the Xiongnu to accept vassal status as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_tributaries_of_Imperial_China" title="List of tributaries of Imperial China">Han tributaries</a>. These campaigns expanded Han sovereignty into the <a href="/wiki/Tarim_Basin" title="Tarim Basin">Tarim Basin</a> of <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, divided the Xiongnu into two separate confederations, and helped establish the vast trade network known as the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a>, which reached as far as the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Mediterranean_region" title="History of the Mediterranean region">Mediterranean world</a>. The territories north of Han\'s borders were quickly overrun by the nomadic <a href="/wiki/Xianbei" title="Xianbei">Xianbei</a> confederation. Emperor Wu also launched successful <a href="/wiki/Southward_expansion_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="Southward expansion of the Han dynasty">military expeditions in the south</a>, annexing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han%E2%80%93Nanyue_War" title="Han&#8211;Nanyue War">Nanyue in 111 BC</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_campaigns_against_Dian" title="Han campaigns against Dian">Dian in 109 BC</a>, and in the <a href="/wiki/Korean_Peninsula" title="Korean Peninsula">Korean Peninsula</a> where the <a href="/wiki/Xuantu_Commandery" title="Xuantu Commandery">Xuantu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lelang_Commandery" title="Lelang Commandery">Lelang Commanderies</a> were established in 108 BC. After 92 AD, the palace <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eunuch_(court_official)" title="Eunuch (court official)">eunuchs</a> increasingly involved themselves in court politics, engaging in violent power struggles between the various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Consort_clan" title="Consort clan">consort clans</a> of the empresses and <a href="/wiki/Empress_dowager" title="Empress dowager">empresses dowager</a>, causing the Han\'s ultimate downfall. Imperial authority was also seriously challenged by large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daoist" title="Daoist">Daoist</a> religious societies which instigated the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Turban_Rebellion" title="Yellow Turban Rebellion">Yellow Turban Rebellion</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Way_of_the_Five_Pecks_of_Rice" title="Way of the Five Pecks of Rice">Five Pecks of Rice Rebellion</a>. Following the death of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Ling_of_Han" title="Emperor Ling of Han">Emperor Ling</a> (r.&#160;168&#8211;189 AD), the palace eunuchs <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="History of the Han dynasty">suffered wholesale massacre</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_history_of_China_(pre-1911)" title="Military history of China (pre-1911)">military officers</a>, allowing members of the aristocracy and military governors to become warlords and <a href="/wiki/End_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="End of the Han dynasty">divide the empire</a>. When <a href="/wiki/Cao_Pi" title="Cao Pi">Cao Pi</a>, King of <a href="/wiki/Cao_Wei" title="Cao Wei">Wei</a>, usurped the throne from <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Xian_of_Han" title="Emperor Xian of Han">Emperor Xian</a>, the Han dynasty ceased to exist.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>According to the <i><a href="/wiki/Records_of_the_Grand_Historian" title="Records of the Grand Historian">Records of the Grand Historian</a></i>, after the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin dynasty</a> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hegemon" title="Hegemon">hegemon</a> <a href="/wiki/Xiang_Yu" title="Xiang Yu">Xiang Yu</a> appointed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liu_Bang" title="Liu Bang">Liu Bang</a> as prince of the small fief of <a href="/wiki/Hanzhong" title="Hanzhong">Hanzhong</a>, named after its location on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_River_(Shaanxi_and_Hubei)" title="Han River (Shaanxi and Hubei)">Han River</a> (in modern southwest <a href="/wiki/Shaanxi" title="Shaanxi">Shaanxi</a>). Following Liu Bang\'s victory in the <a href="/wiki/Chu%E2%80%93Han_Contention" title="Chu&#8211;Han Contention">Chu&#8211;Han Contention</a>, the resulting Han dynasty was named after the Hanzhong fief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELoewe1986116_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoewe1986116-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="History of the Han dynasty">History of the Han dynasty</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="Timeline of the Han dynasty">Timeline of the Han dynasty</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_Han">Western Han</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Western Han">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Han%E2%80%93Xiongnu_War" title="Han&#8211;Xiongnu War">Han&#8211;Xiongnu War</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southward_expansion_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="Southward expansion of the Han dynasty">Southward expansion</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Loulan_Kingdom" title="Loulan Kingdom">Loulan Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shule_Kingdom" title="Shule Kingdom">Shule Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Khotan" title="Kingdom of Khotan">Kingdom of Khotan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saka" title="Saka">Saka</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tocharians" title="Tocharians">Tocharians</a></div>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:308px;max-width:308px"><div style="float:left;margin:1px;width:146px;max-width:146px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bronze_mirror_with_painted_designs,_Western_Han.jpg"><img alt="" decorated with raised <a href="/wiki/Relief" title="Relief">reliefs</a> of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_dragon" title="Chinese dragon">dragons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fenghuang" title="Fenghuang">phoenixes</a>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Taotie" title="Taotie">taotie</a></i><br/> <b>Right image</b>: Reverse side of a Western-Han <a href="/wiki/TLV_mirror" title="TLV mirror">bronze mirror</a> with painted designs of a flower motif</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>\'s first <a href="/wiki/Dynasties_in_Chinese_history" title="Dynasties in Chinese history">imperial dynasty</a> was the <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin dynasty</a> (221&#8211;207 BC). The Qin unified the Chinese <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States</a> by conquest, but their empire became unstable after the death of the first emperor <a href="/wiki/Qin_Shi_Huang" title="Qin Shi Huang">Qin Shi Huang</a>. Within four years, the dynasty\'s authority had collapsed in the face of rebellion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey199960&#8211;61_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey199960&#8211;61-9">[9]</a></sup> Two former rebel leaders, <a href="/wiki/Xiang_Yu" title="Xiang Yu">Xiang Yu</a> (d.&#160;202 BC) of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Chu" title="Kingdom of Chu">Chu</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liu_Bang" title="Liu Bang">Liu Bang</a> (d.&#160;195 BC) of <a href="/wiki/Hanzhong" title="Hanzhong">Han</a>, engaged <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chu-Han_contention" title="Chu-Han contention">in a war</a> to decide who would become <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/hegemony" title="wikt:hegemony">hegemon</a> of China, which had fissured into <a href="/wiki/Eighteen_Kingdoms" title="Eighteen Kingdoms">18 kingdoms</a>, each claiming allegiance to either Xiang Yu or Liu Bang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELoewe1986116&#8211;122_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoewe1986116&#8211;122-10">[10]</a></sup> Although Xiang Yu proved to be a capable commander, Liu Bang defeated him at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gaixia" title="Battle of Gaixia">Battle of Gaixia</a> (202 BC), in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Anhui" title="Anhui">Anhui</a>. Liu Bang assumed the title <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_China" title="Emperor of China">"emperor"</a> (<i>huangdi</i>) at the urging of his followers and is known posthumously as <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Gaozu_of_Han" title="Emperor Gaozu of Han">Emperor Gaozu</a> (r.&#160;202&#8211;195 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavis200144&#8211;46_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavis200144&#8211;46-11">[11]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an" title="Chang\'an">Chang\'an</a> (known today as Xi\'an) was chosen as the new capital of the reunified empire under Han.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELoewe1986122_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoewe1986122-12">[12]</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the beginning of the <b>Western Han</b> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%A5%BF%E6%BC%A2" title="wikt:&#35199;&#28450;">&#35199;&#28450;</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%A5%BF%E6%B1%89" title="wikt:&#35199;&#27721;">&#35199;&#27721;</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">X&#299;h&#224;n</span></i>), also known as the <b>Former Han</b> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%89%8D%E6%BC%A2" title="wikt:&#21069;&#28450;">&#21069;&#28450;</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%89%8D%E6%B1%89" title="wikt:&#21069;&#27721;">&#21069;&#27721;</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Qi&#225;nh&#224;n</span></i>) dynasty, thirteen centrally controlled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commandery_(China)" title="Commandery (China)">commanderies</a>&#8212;including the capital region&#8212;existed in the western third of the empire, while the eastern two-thirds were divided into ten <a href="/wiki/Kings_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="Kings of the Han dynasty">semi-autonomous kingdoms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELoewe1986122&#8211;125_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoewe1986122&#8211;125-13">[13]</a></sup> To placate his prominent commanders from the war with Chu, Emperor Gaozu <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enfeoffment" title="Enfeoffment">enfeoffed</a> some of them as kings. By 157 BC, the Han court had replaced all of these kings with royal <a href="/wiki/Liu" title="Liu">Liu</a> family members, since the loyalty of non-relatives to the throne was questioned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELoewe1986122&#8211;125_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoewe1986122&#8211;125-13">[13]</a></sup> After several insurrections by Han kings&#8212;the largest being the <a href="/wiki/Rebellion_of_the_Seven_States" title="Rebellion of the Seven States">Rebellion of the Seven States</a> in 154 BC&#8212;the imperial court enacted a series of reforms beginning in 145 BC limiting the size and power of these kingdoms and dividing their former territories into new centrally controlled commanderies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELoewe1986139&#8211;144_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoewe1986139&#8211;144-14">[14]</a></sup> Kings were no longer able to appoint their own staff; this duty was assumed by the imperial court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bielenstein_1980_106_chu_1972_76_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-bielenstein_1980_106_chu_1972_76-15">[15]</a></sup> Kings became nominal heads of their <a href="/wiki/Fief" title="Fief">fiefs</a> and collected a portion of tax revenues as their personal incomes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bielenstein_1980_106_chu_1972_76_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-bielenstein_1980_106_chu_1972_76-15">[15]</a></sup> The kingdoms were never entirely abolished and existed throughout the remainder of Western and Eastern Han.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBielenstein1980105_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBielenstein1980105-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Han_map.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="850" decoding="async" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Han_map.jpg/200px-Han_map.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Han_map.jpg/300px-Han_map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Han_map.jpg/400px-Han_map.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Han dynasty in 87 BC</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:End_of_Han_Dynasty_Warlords.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="902" data-file-width="1275" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/End_of_Han_Dynasty_Warlords.png/200px-End_of_Han_Dynasty_Warlords.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/End_of_Han_Dynasty_Warlords.png/300px-End_of_Han_Dynasty_Warlords.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/End_of_Han_Dynasty_Warlords.png/400px-End_of_Han_Dynasty_Warlords.png 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">At the end of Han dynasty in 189&#8211;220 AD</div></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ChangXingongdeng.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1424" data-file-width="1120" decoding="async" height="280" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/ChangXingongdeng.jpg/220px-ChangXingongdeng.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/ChangXingongdeng.jpg/330px-ChangXingongdeng.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/ChangXingongdeng.jpg/440px-ChangXingongdeng.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ChangXingongdeng.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Gilding" title="Gilding">gilded</a> bronze <a href="/wiki/Oil_lamp" title="Oil lamp">oil lamp</a> in the shape of a kneeling female servant, dated 2nd century BC, found in the tomb of <a href="/wiki/Dou_Wan" title="Dou Wan">Dou Wan</a>, wife of the Han prince <a href="/wiki/Liu_Sheng,_Prince_of_Zhongshan" title="Liu Sheng, Prince of Zhongshan">Liu Sheng</a>; its sliding shutter allows for adjustments in the direction and brightness in light while it also traps smoke within the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>To the north of <a href="/wiki/China_proper" title="China proper">China proper</a>, the nomadic <a href="/wiki/Xiongnu" title="Xiongnu">Xiongnu</a> chieftain <a href="/wiki/Modu_Chanyu" title="Modu Chanyu">Modu Chanyu</a> (r.&#160;209&#8211;174 BC) conquered various tribes inhabiting the eastern portion of the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Steppe" title="Eurasian Steppe">Eurasian Steppe</a>. By the end of his reign, he controlled <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Tarim_Basin" title="Tarim Basin">Tarim Basin</a>, subjugating over twenty states east of <a href="/wiki/Samarkand" title="Samarkand">Samarkand</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Emperor Gaozu was troubled about the abundant Han-manufactured iron weapons traded to the Xiongnu along the northern borders, and he established a trade <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Embargo" title="Embargo">embargo</a> against the group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETorday199775&#8211;77_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETorday199775&#8211;77-19">[19]</a></sup> In retaliation, the Xiongnu invaded what is now <a href="/wiki/Shanxi" title="Shanxi">Shanxi</a> province, where they <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Baideng" title="Battle of Baideng">defeated the Han forces at Baideng</a> in 200 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> After negotiations, the <i><a href="/wiki/Heqin" title="Heqin">heqin</a></i> agreement in 198 BC nominally held the leaders of the Xiongnu and the Han as equal partners in a royal marriage alliance, but the Han were forced to send large amounts of tribute items such as <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a> clothes, food, and wine to the Xiongnu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mawangdui_silk_banner_from_tomb_no1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2250" data-file-width="903" decoding="async" height="374" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Mawangdui_silk_banner_from_tomb_no1.jpg/150px-Mawangdui_silk_banner_from_tomb_no1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Mawangdui_silk_banner_from_tomb_no1.jpg/225px-Mawangdui_silk_banner_from_tomb_no1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Mawangdui_silk_banner_from_tomb_no1.jpg/300px-Mawangdui_silk_banner_from_tomb_no1.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mawangdui_silk_banner_from_tomb_no1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/History_of_silk" title="History of silk">silk banner</a> from <a href="/wiki/Mawangdui" title="Mawangdui">Mawangdui</a>, <a href="/wiki/Changsha" title="Changsha">Changsha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hunan" title="Hunan">Hunan</a> province. It was draped over the coffin of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lady_Dai" title="Lady Dai">Lady Dai</a> (d.&#160;168 BC), wife of the Marquess Li Cang (&#21033;&#33980;) (d.&#160;186 BC), chancellor for the <a href="/wiki/Kings_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="Kings of the Han dynasty">Kingdom of Changsha</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHansen2000117&#8211;119_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHansen2000117&#8211;119-22">[22]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Despite the tribute and a negotiation between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laoshang_Chanyu" title="Laoshang Chanyu">Laoshang Chanyu</a> (r.&#160;174&#8211;160 BC) and <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wen_of_Han" title="Emperor Wen of Han">Emperor Wen</a> (r.&#160;180&#8211;157 BC) to reopen border markets, many of the <a href="/wiki/Chanyu" title="Chanyu">Chanyu</a>\'s Xiongnu subordinates chose not to obey the treaty and periodically raided Han territories south of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Wall_of_China" title="Great Wall of China">Great Wall</a> for additional goods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> In a court conference assembled by <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wu_of_Han" title="Emperor Wu of Han">Emperor Wu</a> (r.&#160;141&#8211;87 BC) in 135 BC, the <a href="/wiki/Consensus_decision-making" title="Consensus decision-making">majority consensus</a> of the ministers was to retain the <i>heqin</i> agreement. Emperor Wu accepted this, despite continuing Xiongnu raids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> However, a court conference the following year convinced the majority that a <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mayi" title="Battle of Mayi">limited engagement at Mayi</a> involving the assassination of the Chanyu would throw the Xiongnu realm into chaos and benefit the Han.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> When this plot failed in 133 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETorday199791&#8211;92_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETorday199791&#8211;92-26">[26]</a></sup> Emperor Wu launched a series of <a href="/wiki/Han%E2%80%93Xiongnu_War" title="Han&#8211;Xiongnu War">massive military invasions</a> into Xiongnu territory. The assault culminated in 119 BC at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mobei" title="Battle of Mobei">Battle of Mobei</a>, where the Han commanders <a href="/wiki/Huo_Qubing" title="Huo Qubing">Huo Qubing</a> (d.&#160;117 BC) and <a href="/wiki/Wei_Qing" title="Wei Qing">Wei Qing</a> (d.&#160;106 BC) forced the Xiongnu court to flee north of the <a href="/wiki/Gobi_Desert" title="Gobi Desert">Gobi Desert</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After Wu\'s reign, Han forces continued to prevail against the Xiongnu. The Xiongnu leader Huhanye Chanyu (&#21628;&#38867;&#37034;) (r.&#160;58&#8211;31 BC) finally submitted to Han as a tributary vassal in 51 BC. His rival claimant to the throne, <a href="/wiki/Zhizhi_Chanyu" title="Zhizhi Chanyu">Zhizhi Chanyu</a> (r.&#160;56&#8211;36 BC), was killed by <a href="/wiki/Chen_Tang" title="Chen Tang">Chen Tang</a> and Gan Yanshou (&#29976;&#24310;&#22781;/&#29976;&#24310;&#23551;) at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Zhizhi" title="Battle of Zhizhi">Battle of Zhizhi</a>, in modern <a href="/wiki/Taraz" title="Taraz">Taraz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 121 BC, Han forces expelled the Xiongnu from a vast territory spanning the <a href="/wiki/Hexi_Corridor" title="Hexi Corridor">Hexi Corridor</a> to <a href="/wiki/Lop_Nur" title="Lop Nur">Lop Nur</a>. They repelled a joint Xiongnu-<a href="/wiki/Qiang_people" title="Qiang people">Qiang</a> invasion of this northwestern territory in 111 BC. In that year, the Han court established four new frontier commanderies in this region: <a href="/wiki/Jiuquan" title="Jiuquan">Jiuquan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhangyi" title="Zhangyi">Zhangyi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dunhuang" title="Dunhuang">Dunhuang</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wuwei,_Gansu" title="Wuwei, Gansu">Wuwei</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> The majority of people on the frontier were soldiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChang200734&#8211;35_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChang200734&#8211;35-30">[30]</a></sup> On occasion, the court forcibly moved peasant farmers to new frontier settlements, along with government-owned slaves and convicts who performed hard labor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChang20076,_15&#8211;16,_44&#8211;45_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChang20076,_15&#8211;16,_44&#8211;45-31">[31]</a></sup> The court also encouraged <a href="/wiki/Commoner" title="Commoner">commoners</a>, such as farmers, merchants, landowners, and hired laborers, to voluntarily migrate to the frontier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChang200715&#8211;16,_33&#8211;35,_42&#8211;43_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChang200715&#8211;16,_33&#8211;35,_42&#8211;43-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Even before Han\'s expansion into Central Asia, diplomat <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Qian" title="Zhang Qian">Zhang Qian</a>\'s travels from 139 to 125 BC had established Chinese contacts with many surrounding civilizations. Zhang encountered <a href="/wiki/Dayuan" title="Dayuan">Dayuan</a> (<a href="/wiki/Fergana" title="Fergana">Fergana</a>), <a href="/wiki/Kangju" title="Kangju">Kangju</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sogdiana" title="Sogdiana">Sogdiana</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Daxia" title="Daxia">Daxia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a>, formerly the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian_Kingdom" title="Greco-Bactrian Kingdom">Greco-Bactrian Kingdom</a>); he also gathered information on Shendu (<a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus River</a> valley of <a href="/wiki/North_India" title="North India">North India</a>) and Anxi (the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a>). All of these countries eventually received Han embassies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> These connections marked the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> trade network that extended to the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, bringing <a href="/wiki/History_of_silk" title="History of silk">Han items like silk</a> to Rome and <a href="/wiki/Roman_glass" title="Roman glass">Roman goods such as glasswares</a> to China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>\n</p><p>From roughly 115 to 60 BC, Han forces fought the Xiongnu over control of the oasis <a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">city-states</a> in the Tarim Basin. Han was eventually victorious and established the <a href="/wiki/Protectorate_of_the_Western_Regions" title="Protectorate of the Western Regions">Protectorate of the Western Regions</a> in 60 BC, which dealt with the region\'s defense and foreign affairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The Han also <a href="/wiki/Southward_expansion_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="Southward expansion of the Han dynasty">expanded southward</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han%E2%80%93Nanyue_War" title="Han&#8211;Nanyue War">naval conquest of Nanyue</a> in 111 BC expanded the Han realm into what are now modern <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">Guangdong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guangxi" title="Guangxi">Guangxi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Northern_and_southern_Vietnam" title="Northern and southern Vietnam">northern Vietnam</a>. <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan</a> was brought into the Han realm with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_campaigns_against_Dian" title="Han campaigns against Dian">conquest</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Dian_Kingdom" title="Dian Kingdom">Dian Kingdom</a> in 109 BC, followed by parts of the <a href="/wiki/Korean_Peninsula" title="Korean Peninsula">Korean Peninsula</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Han_conquest_of_Gojoseon" title="Han conquest of Gojoseon">Han conquest of Gojoseon</a> and colonial establishments of <a href="/wiki/Xuantu_Commandery" title="Xuantu Commandery">Xuantu Commandery</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lelang_Commandery" title="Lelang Commandery">Lelang Commandery</a> in 108 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> In China\'s first known nationwide <a href="/wiki/Census" title="Census">census</a> taken in 2&#160;AD, the population was registered as having 57,671,400 individuals in 12,366,470 households.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENishijima1986595&#8211;596_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENishijima1986595&#8211;596-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p><p>To pay for his military campaigns and colonial expansion, Emperor Wu <a href="/wiki/Nationalization" title="Nationalization">nationalized</a> several private industries. He created central government <a href="/wiki/Monopoly" title="Monopoly">monopolies</a> administered largely by <a href="/wiki/Four_occupations" title="Four occupations">former merchants</a>. These monopolies included salt, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_ferrous_metallurgy" title="History of ferrous metallurgy">iron</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_alcoholic_beverages" title="Chinese alcoholic beverages">liquor</a> production, as well as <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Chinese_coinage" title="Ancient Chinese coinage">bronze-coin currency</a>. The liquor monopoly lasted only from 98 to 81 BC, and the salt and iron monopolies were eventually abolished in early Eastern Han. The issuing of coinage remained a central government monopoly throughout the rest of the Han dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The government monopolies were eventually repealed when a political faction known as the Reformists gained greater influence in the court. The Reformists opposed the Modernist faction that had dominated court politics in Emperor Wu\'s reign and during the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Regent" title="Regent">regency</a> of <a href="/wiki/Huo_Guang" title="Huo Guang">Huo Guang</a> (d.&#160;68 BC). The Modernists argued for an aggressive and expansionary foreign policy supported by revenues from heavy government intervention in the private economy. The Reformists, however, overturned these policies, favoring a cautious, non-expansionary approach to foreign policy, frugal <a href="/wiki/Government_budget" title="Government budget">budget</a> reform, and lower tax-rates imposed on private entrepreneurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Wang_Mang.27s_reign_and_civil_war"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Wang_Mang\'s_reign_and_civil_war">Wang Mang\'s reign and civil war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Wang Mang\'s reign and civil war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Wang_Mang" title="Wang Mang">Wang Mang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xin_dynasty" title="Xin dynasty">Xin dynasty</a></div>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:308px;max-width:308px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:145px;max-width:145px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_painted_figure_of_a_cavalryman.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2475" data-file-width="2475" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_painted_figure_of_a_cavalryman.jpg/143px-CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_painted_figure_of_a_cavalryman.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_painted_figure_of_a_cavalryman.jpg/215px-CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_painted_figure_of_a_cavalryman.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_painted_figure_of_a_cavalryman.jpg/286px-CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_painted_figure_of_a_cavalryman.jpg 2x" width="143"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:159px;max-width:159px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bronze_horse_with_lead_saddle,_Han_Dynasty.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="2522" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Bronze_horse_with_lead_saddle%2C_Han_Dynasty.jpg/157px-Bronze_horse_with_lead_saddle%2C_Han_Dynasty.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Bronze_horse_with_lead_saddle%2C_Han_Dynasty.jpg/236px-Bronze_horse_with_lead_saddle%2C_Han_Dynasty.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Bronze_horse_with_lead_saddle%2C_Han_Dynasty.jpg/314px-Bronze_horse_with_lead_saddle%2C_Han_Dynasty.jpg 2x" width="157"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><b>Left image</b>: A Western-Han painted ceramic mounted cavalryman from the tomb of a military general at <a href="/wiki/Xianyang" title="Xianyang">Xianyang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shaanxi" title="Shaanxi">Shaanxi</a><br/> <b>Right image</b>: A Western or Eastern Han <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a> horse statuette with a lead saddle</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Wang_Zhengjun" title="Wang Zhengjun">Wang Zhengjun</a> (71 BC&#8211;13 AD) was first empress, then <a href="/wiki/Empress_dowager" title="Empress dowager">empress dowager</a>, and finally <a href="/wiki/Grand_empress_dowager" title="Grand empress dowager">grand empress dowager</a> during the reigns of the Emperors <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Yuan_of_Han" title="Emperor Yuan of Han">Yuan</a> (r.&#160;49&#8211;33 BC), <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Cheng_of_Han" title="Emperor Cheng of Han">Cheng</a> (r.&#160;33&#8211;7 BC), and <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Ai_of_Han" title="Emperor Ai of Han">Ai</a> (r.&#160;7&#8211;1 BC), respectively. During this time, a succession of her male relatives held the title of regent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Following the death of Ai, Wang Zhengjun\'s nephew <a href="/wiki/Wang_Mang" title="Wang Mang">Wang Mang</a> (45 BC&#8211;23 AD) was appointed regent as Marshall of State on 16 August under <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Ping_of_Han" title="Emperor Ping of Han">Emperor Ping</a> (r. 1 BC&#8211;6 AD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBielenstein1986227&#8211;230_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBielenstein1986227&#8211;230-40">[40]</a></sup> When Ping died on 3 February 6 AD, <a href="/wiki/Ruzi_Ying" title="Ruzi Ying">Ruzi Ying</a> (d. 25 AD) was chosen as the heir and Wang Mang was appointed to serve as acting emperor for the child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBielenstein1986227&#8211;230_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBielenstein1986227&#8211;230-40">[40]</a></sup> Wang promised to relinquish his control to Liu Ying once he came of age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBielenstein1986227&#8211;230_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBielenstein1986227&#8211;230-40">[40]</a></sup> Despite this promise, and against protest and revolts from the nobility, Wang Mang claimed on 10 January that the divine <a href="/wiki/Mandate_of_Heaven" title="Mandate of Heaven">Mandate of Heaven</a> called for the end of the Han dynasty and the beginning of his own: the <a href="/wiki/Xin_dynasty" title="Xin dynasty">Xin dynasty</a> (9&#8211;23 AD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Wang Mang initiated a series of major reforms that were ultimately unsuccessful. These reforms included <a href="/wiki/History_of_slavery" title="History of slavery">outlawing slavery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nationalization" title="Nationalization">nationalizing</a> land to <a href="/wiki/Well-field_system" title="Well-field system">equally distribute</a> between households, and introducing new currencies, a change which debased the value of coinage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Although these reforms provoked considerable opposition, Wang\'s regime met its ultimate downfall with the massive floods of c. 3 AD and 11 AD. Gradual silt buildup in the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_River" title="Yellow River">Yellow River</a> had raised its water level and overwhelmed the <a href="/wiki/Flood_control" title="Flood control">flood control works</a>. The Yellow River split into two new branches: one emptying to the north and the other to the south of the <a href="/wiki/Shandong_Peninsula" title="Shandong Peninsula">Shandong Peninsula</a>, though Han engineers managed to dam the southern branch by 70 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hansen_2000_135_de_Crespigny_2007_196_bielenstein_1986_241_244_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-hansen_2000_135_de_Crespigny_2007_196_bielenstein_1986_241_244-43">[43]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The flood dislodged thousands of peasant farmers, many of whom joined roving bandit and rebel groups such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_Eyebrows_Rebellions" title="Red Eyebrows Rebellions">Red Eyebrows</a> to survive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hansen_2000_135_de_Crespigny_2007_196_bielenstein_1986_241_244_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-hansen_2000_135_de_Crespigny_2007_196_bielenstein_1986_241_244-43">[43]</a></sup> Wang Mang\'s armies were incapable of quelling these enlarged rebel groups. Eventually, an insurgent mob forced their way into the <a href="/wiki/Weiyang_Palace" title="Weiyang Palace">Weiyang Palace</a> and killed Wang Mang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:S-148_Wang_Mang_huo_bu.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="451" data-file-width="412" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/S-148_Wang_Mang_huo_bu.JPG/170px-S-148_Wang_Mang_huo_bu.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/S-148_Wang_Mang_huo_bu.JPG/255px-S-148_Wang_Mang_huo_bu.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/S-148_Wang_Mang_huo_bu.JPG/340px-S-148_Wang_Mang_huo_bu.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:S-148_Wang_Mang_huo_bu.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A spade-shaped bronze coin issued during <a href="/wiki/Wang_Mang" title="Wang Mang">Wang Mang</a>\'s (r. 9&#8211;23 AD) reign</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Gengshi_Emperor" title="Gengshi Emperor">Gengshi Emperor</a> (r.&#160;23&#8211;25 AD), a descendant of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Jing_of_Han" title="Emperor Jing of Han">Emperor Jing</a> (r.&#160;157&#8211;141 BC), attempted to restore the Han dynasty and occupied Chang\'an as his capital. However, he was overwhelmed by the Red Eyebrow rebels who deposed, assassinated, and replaced him with the puppet monarch <a href="/wiki/Liu_Penzi" title="Liu Penzi">Liu Penzi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Gengshi\'s brother Liu Xiu, known posthumously as <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Guangwu_of_Han" title="Emperor Guangwu of Han">Emperor Guangwu</a> (r.&#160;25&#8211;57 AD), after distinguishing himself at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kunyang" title="Battle of Kunyang">Battle of Kunyang</a> in 23 AD, was urged to succeed Gengshi as emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Under Guangwu\'s rule the Han Empire was restored. Guangwu made <a href="/wiki/Luoyang" title="Luoyang">Luoyang</a> his capital in 25 AD, and by 27 AD his officers <a href="/wiki/Deng_Yu" title="Deng Yu">Deng Yu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Feng_Yi" title="Feng Yi">Feng Yi</a> had forced the Red Eyebrows to surrender and executed their leaders for <a href="/wiki/Treason" title="Treason">treason</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> From 26 until 36 AD, Emperor Guangwu had to wage war against other regional warlords who claimed the title of emperor; when these warlords were defeated, China reunified under the Han.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The period between the foundation of the Han dynasty and Wang Mang\'s reign is known as the Western Han (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%A5%BF%E6%BC%A2" title="wikt:&#35199;&#28450;">&#35199;&#28450;</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%A5%BF%E6%B1%89" title="wikt:&#35199;&#27721;">&#35199;&#27721;</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">X&#299;h&#224;n</span></i>) or Former Han (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%89%8D%E6%BC%A2" title="wikt:&#21069;&#28450;">&#21069;&#28450;</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%89%8D%E6%B1%89" title="wikt:&#21069;&#27721;">&#21069;&#27721;</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Qi&#225;nh&#224;n</span></i>) (206 BC&#8211;9 AD). During this period the capital was at <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an" title="Chang\'an">Chang\'an</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi\'an">Xi\'an</a>). From the reign of Guangwu the capital was moved eastward to Luoyang. The era from his reign until <a href="/wiki/End_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="End of the Han dynasty">the fall of Han</a> is known as the Eastern Han or Later Han (25&#8211;220 AD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHinsch200224&#8211;25_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHinsch200224&#8211;25-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eastern_Han">Eastern Han</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Eastern Han">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%E9%87%91%E9%A5%BC_%E6%B5%B7%E6%98%8F%E4%BE%AF%E5%9B%BD%E9%81%97%E5%9D%80.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2160" data-file-width="3840" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/%E9%87%91%E9%A5%BC_%E6%B5%B7%E6%98%8F%E4%BE%AF%E5%9B%BD%E9%81%97%E5%9D%80.JPG/220px-%E9%87%91%E9%A5%BC_%E6%B5%B7%E6%98%8F%E4%BE%AF%E5%9B%BD%E9%81%97%E5%9D%80.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/%E9%87%91%E9%A5%BC_%E6%B5%B7%E6%98%8F%E4%BE%AF%E5%9B%BD%E9%81%97%E5%9D%80.JPG/330px-%E9%87%91%E9%A5%BC_%E6%B5%B7%E6%98%8F%E4%BE%AF%E5%9B%BD%E9%81%97%E5%9D%80.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/%E9%87%91%E9%A5%BC_%E6%B5%B7%E6%98%8F%E4%BE%AF%E5%9B%BD%E9%81%97%E5%9D%80.JPG/440px-%E9%87%91%E9%A5%BC_%E6%B5%B7%E6%98%8F%E4%BE%AF%E5%9B%BD%E9%81%97%E5%9D%80.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%E9%87%91%E9%A5%BC_%E6%B5%B7%E6%98%8F%E4%BE%AF%E5%9B%BD%E9%81%97%E5%9D%80.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gold Coins of the Eastern Han Dynasty</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Early_Eastern_Han_Warlords.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="796" data-file-width="1165" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Early_Eastern_Han_Warlords.png/300px-Early_Eastern_Han_Warlords.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Early_Eastern_Han_Warlords.png/450px-Early_Eastern_Han_Warlords.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Early_Eastern_Han_Warlords.png/600px-Early_Eastern_Han_Warlords.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Early_Eastern_Han_Warlords.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Situation of warlords and peasant forces at the beginning of Eastern Han dynasty</div></div></div>\n<p>The <b>Eastern Han</b> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%9D%B1%E6%BC%A2" title="wikt:&#26481;&#28450;">&#26481;&#28450;</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E4%B8%9C%E6%B1%89" title="wikt:&#19996;&#27721;">&#19996;&#27721;</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">D&#333;ngh&#224;n</span></i>), also known as the <b>Later Han</b> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">traditional Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%BE%8C%E6%BC%A2" title="wikt:&#24460;&#28450;">&#24460;&#28450;</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%90%8E%E6%B1%89" title="wikt:&#21518;&#27721;">&#21518;&#27721;</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">H&#242;uh&#224;n</span></i>), formally began on 5 August 25, when Liu Xiu became <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Guangwu_of_Han" title="Emperor Guangwu of Han">Emperor Guangwu of Han</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKnechtges2010116_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKnechtges2010116-50">[50]</a></sup> During the widespread rebellion against Wang Mang, the state of <a href="/wiki/Goguryeo" title="Goguryeo">Goguryeo</a> was free to raid Han\'s <a href="/wiki/Four_Commanderies_of_Han" title="Four Commanderies of Han">Korean commanderies</a>; Han did not reaffirm its control over the region until AD 30.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEY&#252;1986450_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEY&#252;1986450-51">[51]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tr%C6%B0ng_Sisters" title="Tr&#432;ng Sisters">Tr&#432;ng Sisters</a> of <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> rebelled against Han in AD 40. Their rebellion was crushed by Han general <a href="/wiki/Ma_Yuan_(Han_dynasty)" title="Ma Yuan (Han dynasty)">Ma Yuan</a> (d.&#160;AD 49) in a campaign from AD 42&#8211;43.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Wang Mang renewed hostilities against the <a href="/wiki/Xiongnu" title="Xiongnu">Xiongnu</a>, who were estranged from Han until their leader Bi (&#27604;), a rival claimant to the throne against his cousin Punu (&#33970;&#22900;), submitted to Han as a tributary vassal in AD 50. This created two rival Xiongnu states: the Southern Xiongnu led by Bi, an ally of Han, and the Northern Xiongnu led by Punu, an enemy of Han.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the turbulent reign of Wang Mang, China lost control over the Tarim Basin, which was conquered by the Northern Xiongnu in AD 63 and used as a base to invade the Hexi Corridor in <a href="/wiki/Gansu" title="Gansu">Gansu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEY&#252;1986413&#8211;414_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEY&#252;1986413&#8211;414-54">[54]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dou_Gu" title="Dou Gu">Dou Gu</a> (d.&#160;88 AD) defeated the Northern Xiongnu at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Yiwulu" title="Battle of Yiwulu">Battle of Yiwulu</a> in AD 73, evicting them from <a href="/wiki/Turpan" title="Turpan">Turpan</a> and chasing them as far as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barkol_Kazakh_Autonomous_County" title="Barkol Kazakh Autonomous County">Lake Barkol</a> before establishing a garrison <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hami_Prefecture" title="Hami Prefecture">at Hami</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEY&#252;1986414&#8211;415_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEY&#252;1986414&#8211;415-55">[55]</a></sup> After the new Protector General of the Western Regions <a href="/wiki/Chen_Mu" title="Chen Mu">Chen Mu</a> (d.&#160;AD 75) was killed by allies of the Xiongnu in <a href="/wiki/Karasahr" title="Karasahr">Karasahr</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kucha" title="Kucha">Kucha</a>, the garrison at Hami was withdrawn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> At the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ikh_Bayan" title="Battle of Ikh Bayan">Battle of Ikh Bayan</a> in AD 89, <a href="/wiki/Dou_Xian" title="Dou Xian">Dou Xian</a> (d.&#160;AD 92) defeated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Chanyu_(1st_century)" title="Northern Chanyu (1st century)">Northern Xiongnu chanyu</a> who then retreated into the <a href="/wiki/Altai_Mountains" title="Altai Mountains">Altai Mountains</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> After the Northern Xiongnu fled into the <a href="/wiki/Ili_River" title="Ili River">Ili River</a> valley in AD 91, the nomadic <a href="/wiki/Xianbei" title="Xianbei">Xianbei</a> occupied the area from the borders of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buyeo_Kingdom" title="Buyeo Kingdom">Buyeo Kingdom</a> in Manchuria to the Ili River of the <a href="/wiki/Wusun" title="Wusun">Wusun</a> people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEY&#252;1986405,_443&#8211;444_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEY&#252;1986405,_443&#8211;444-58">[58]</a></sup> The Xianbei reached their apogee under Tanshihuai (&#27264;&#30707;&#27088;) (d.&#160;AD 180), who consistently defeated Chinese armies. However, Tanshihuai\'s confederation disintegrated after his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEY&#252;1986444&#8211;446_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEY&#252;1986444&#8211;446-59">[59]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ban_Chao" title="Ban Chao">Ban Chao</a> (d.&#160;AD 102) enlisted the aid of the <a href="/wiki/Kushan_Empire" title="Kushan Empire">Kushan Empire</a>, occupying the area of modern <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>, to subdue <a href="/wiki/Kashgar" title="Kashgar">Kashgar</a> and its ally Sogdiana.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-torday_1997_393_de_Crespigny_2007_5_6_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-torday_1997_393_de_Crespigny_2007_5_6-60">[60]</a></sup> When a request by Kushan ruler <a href="/wiki/Vima_Kadphises" title="Vima Kadphises">Vima Kadphises</a> (r.&#160;c.&#160;90&#8211;c.&#160;100 AD) for a marriage alliance with the Han was rejected in AD 90, he sent his forces to <a href="/wiki/Wakhan" title="Wakhan">Wakhan</a> (Afghanistan) to attack Ban Chao. The conflict ended with the Kushans withdrawing because of lack of supplies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-torday_1997_393_de_Crespigny_2007_5_6_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-torday_1997_393_de_Crespigny_2007_5_6-60">[60]</a></sup> In AD 91, the office of Protector General of the Western Regions was reinstated when it was bestowed on Ban Chao.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEY&#252;1986415&#8211;416_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEY&#252;1986415&#8211;416-61">[61]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eastern_Han_ingot_imprints_with_barbarous_Greek_inscriptions.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="402" data-file-width="816" decoding="async" height="108" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Eastern_Han_ingot_imprints_with_barbarous_Greek_inscriptions.jpg/220px-Eastern_Han_ingot_imprints_with_barbarous_Greek_inscriptions.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Eastern_Han_ingot_imprints_with_barbarous_Greek_inscriptions.jpg/330px-Eastern_Han_ingot_imprints_with_barbarous_Greek_inscriptions.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Eastern_Han_ingot_imprints_with_barbarous_Greek_inscriptions.jpg/440px-Eastern_Han_ingot_imprints_with_barbarous_Greek_inscriptions.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eastern_Han_ingot_imprints_with_barbarous_Greek_inscriptions.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eastern Han inscriptions on a lead ingot, using barbarous <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Greek_alphabet" title="History of the Greek alphabet">Greek alphabet</a> in the style of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kushans" title="Kushans">Kushans</a>, excavated in <a href="/wiki/Shaanxi" title="Shaanxi">Shaanxi</a>, 1st&#8211;2nd century AD<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECribb197876&#8211;78_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECribb197876&#8211;78-62">[62]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Foreign travelers to Eastern-Han China include <a href="/wiki/Bhikkhu" title="Bhikkhu">Buddhist monks</a> who <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road_transmission_of_Buddhism" title="Silk Road transmission of Buddhism">translated works into Chinese</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/An_Shigao" title="An Shigao">An Shigao</a> from Parthia, and <a href="/wiki/Lokaksema_(Buddhist_monk)" title="Lokaksema (Buddhist monk)">Lokaksema</a> from Kushan-era <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandhara</a>, India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> In addition to tributary relations with the Kushans, the Han Empire received gifts from the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a>, from a king in modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burma" title="Burma">Burma</a>, from a ruler <a href="/wiki/Wa_(Japan)" title="Wa (Japan)">in Japan</a>, and initiated an unsuccessful mission to <a href="/wiki/Daqin" title="Daqin">Daqin</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Rome</a>) in AD 97 with <a href="/wiki/Gan_Ying" title="Gan Ying">Gan Ying</a> as emissary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Sino-Roman_relations" title="Sino-Roman relations">Roman embassy</a> of Emperor <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a> (r.&#160;161&#8211;180 AD) is recorded in the <i><a href="/wiki/Weil%C3%BCe" title="Weil&#252;e">Weil&#252;e</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hou_Hanshu" title="Hou Hanshu">Hou Hanshu</a></i> to have reached the court of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Huan_of_Han" title="Emperor Huan of Han">Emperor Huan of Han</a> (r.&#160;AD 146&#8211;168) in AD 166,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChavannes1907185_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChavannes1907185-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHill200927_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHill200927-66">[66]</a></sup> yet <a href="/wiki/Rafe_de_Crespigny" title="Rafe de Crespigny">Rafe de Crespigny</a> asserts that this was most likely a group of <a href="/wiki/Roman_commerce" title="Roman commerce">Roman merchants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> In addition to <a href="/wiki/Roman_glass" title="Roman glass">Roman glasswares</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_currency" title="Roman currency">coins</a> found in China,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Roman <a href="/wiki/Medal" title="Medal">medallions</a> from the reign of <a href="/wiki/Antoninus_Pius" title="Antoninus Pius">Antoninus Pius</a> and his adopted son Marcus Aurelius have been found at <a href="/wiki/%C3%93c_Eo" title="&#211;c Eo">&#211;c Eo</a> in Vietnam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> This was near the commandery of <a href="/wiki/Rinan" title="Rinan">Rinan</a> (also <a href="/wiki/Jiaozhi" title="Jiaozhi">Jiaozhi</a>) where Chinese sources claim the Romans first landed, as well as embassies from <a href="/wiki/Tianzhu_(India)" title="Tianzhu (India)">Tianzhu</a> (in northern India) in the years 159 and 161.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> &#211;c Eo is also thought to be the port city "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cattigara" title="Cattigara">Cattigara</a>" described by <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/Geography_(Ptolemy)" title="Geography (Ptolemy)">Geography</a></i> (c. 150 AD) as lying east of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Chersonese" title="Golden Chersonese">Golden Chersonese</a> (<a href="/wiki/Malay_Peninsula" title="Malay Peninsula">Malay Peninsula</a>) along the <i><a href="/wiki/Magnus_Sinus" title="Magnus Sinus">Magnus Sinus</a></i> (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Thailand" title="Gulf of Thailand">Gulf of Thailand</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_China_Sea" title="South China Sea">South China Sea</a>), where <a href="/wiki/Indo-Roman_trade_relations" title="Indo-Roman trade relations">a Greek sailor</a> had visited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gansu_Museum_2007_257.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Gansu_Museum_2007_257.jpg/200px-Gansu_Museum_2007_257.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Gansu_Museum_2007_257.jpg/300px-Gansu_Museum_2007_257.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Gansu_Museum_2007_257.jpg/400px-Gansu_Museum_2007_257.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gansu_Museum_2007_257.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gansu_Flying_Horse" title="Gansu Flying Horse">Gansu Flying Horse</a>, depicted in full gallop, <a href="/wiki/Bronze_sculpture" title="Bronze sculpture">bronze sculpture</a>, h 34.5 cm. <a href="/wiki/Wuwei,_Gansu" title="Wuwei, Gansu">Wuwei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gansu" title="Gansu">Gansu</a>, China, AD 25&#8211;220</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Emperor_Zhang_of_Han" title="Emperor Zhang of Han">Emperor Zhang\'s</a> (r.&#160;75&#8211;88 AD) reign came to be viewed by later Eastern Han scholars as the high point of the dynastic house.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Crespigny2007497,_500,_592_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Crespigny2007497,_500,_592-72">[72]</a></sup> Subsequent reigns were increasingly marked by <a href="/wiki/Eunuch" title="Eunuch">eunuch</a> intervention in court politics and their involvement in the violent power struggles of the imperial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Consort_clan" title="Consort clan">consort clans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> With the aid of the eunuch <a href="/wiki/Zheng_Zhong" title="Zheng Zhong">Zheng Zhong</a> (d. 107 AD), <a href="/wiki/Emperor_He_of_Han" title="Emperor He of Han">Emperor He</a> (r.&#160;88&#8211;105 AD) had <a href="/wiki/Empress_Dou_(Zhang)" title="Empress Dou (Zhang)">Empress Dowager Dou</a> (d.&#160;97 AD) put under <a href="/wiki/House_arrest" title="House arrest">house arrest</a> and her clan stripped of power. This was in revenge for Dou\'s purging of the clan of his natural mother&#8212;<a href="/wiki/Consort_Liang" title="Consort Liang">Consort Liang</a>&#8212;and then concealing her identity from him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> After Emperor He\'s death, his wife <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empress_Deng_Sui" title="Empress Deng Sui">Empress Deng Sui</a> (d.&#160;121 AD) managed state affairs as the regent empress dowager during a turbulent financial crisis and widespread Qiang rebellion that lasted from 107 to 118 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>\n</p><p>When Empress Dowager Deng died, <a href="/wiki/Emperor_An_of_Han" title="Emperor An of Han">Emperor An</a> (r.&#160;106&#8211;125 AD) was convinced by the accusations of the eunuchs Li Run (<span lang="zh-Hant" title="Chinese language text">&#26446;&#38287;</span>) and Jiang Jing (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#27743;&#20140;</span>) that Deng and her family had planned to depose him. An dismissed Deng\'s clan members from office, exiled them and forced many to commit suicide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> After An\'s death, his wife, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empress_Yan_Ji" title="Empress Yan Ji">Empress Dowager Yan</a> (d.&#160;126 AD) placed the child <a href="/wiki/Marquess_of_Beixiang" title="Marquess of Beixiang">Marquess of Beixiang</a> on the throne in an attempt to retain power within her family. However, palace eunuch <a href="/wiki/Sun_Cheng" title="Sun Cheng">Sun Cheng</a> (d.&#160;132 AD) masterminded a successful overthrow of her regime to enthrone <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Shun_of_Han" title="Emperor Shun of Han">Emperor Shun of Han</a> (r.&#160;125&#8211;144 AD). Yan was placed under house arrest, her relatives were either killed or exiled, and her eunuch allies were slaughtered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> The regent <a href="/wiki/Liang_Ji" title="Liang Ji">Liang Ji</a> (d.&#160;159 AD), brother of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empress_Liang_Na" title="Empress Liang Na">Empress Liang Na</a> (d.&#160;150 AD), had the brother-in-law of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empress_Deng_Mengn%C3%BC" title="Empress Deng Mengn&#252;">Consort Deng Mengn&#252;</a> (later empress) (d.&#160;165 AD) killed after Deng Mengn&#252; resisted Liang Ji\'s attempts to control her. Afterward, Emperor Huan employed eunuchs to depose Liang Ji, who was then forced to commit suicide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Han_Dynasty_Granary_west_of_Dunhuang.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1717" data-file-width="4519" decoding="async" height="84" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Han_Dynasty_Granary_west_of_Dunhuang.jpg/220px-Han_Dynasty_Granary_west_of_Dunhuang.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Han_Dynasty_Granary_west_of_Dunhuang.jpg/330px-Han_Dynasty_Granary_west_of_Dunhuang.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Han_Dynasty_Granary_west_of_Dunhuang.jpg/440px-Han_Dynasty_Granary_west_of_Dunhuang.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Han_Dynasty_Granary_west_of_Dunhuang.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>These <a href="/wiki/Rammed_earth" title="Rammed earth">rammed earth</a> ruins of a <a href="/wiki/Granary" title="Granary">granary</a> in Hecang Fortress (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">&#27827;&#20179;&#22478;</span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">H&#233;c&#257;ng ch&#233;ng</span></i>), located ~11 km (7 miles) northeast of the Western-Han-era <a href="/wiki/Yumen_Pass" title="Yumen Pass">Yumen Pass</a>, were built during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Han" title="Western Han">Western Han</a> (202 BC&#8211;9 AD) and significantly rebuilt during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Jin" title="Western Jin">Western Jin</a> (280&#8211;316 AD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWangLiZhang2010351&#8211;352_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWangLiZhang2010351&#8211;352-79">[79]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Students from the <a href="/wiki/Taixue" title="Taixue">Imperial University</a> organized a widespread <a href="/wiki/Student_protest" title="Student protest">student protest</a> against the eunuchs of Emperor Huan\'s court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHansen2000141_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHansen2000141-80">[80]</a></sup> Huan further alienated the bureaucracy when he initiated grandiose construction projects and hosted thousands of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concubine" title="Concubine">concubines</a> in his <a href="/wiki/Harem" title="Harem">harem</a> at a time of economic crisis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Palace eunuchs imprisoned the official Li Ying (<span lang="zh-Hant" title="Chinese language text">&#26446;&#33210;</span>) and his associates from the Imperial University on a dubious charge of treason. In 167 AD, the Grand Commandant <a href="/wiki/Dou_Wu" title="Dou Wu">Dou Wu</a> (d.&#160;168 AD) convinced his son-in-law, Emperor Huan, to release them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Crespigny2007602_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Crespigny2007602-82">[82]</a></sup> However the emperor permanently barred Li Ying and his associates from serving in office, marking the beginning of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disasters_of_Partisan_Prohibitions" title="Disasters of Partisan Prohibitions">Partisan Prohibitions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Crespigny2007602_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Crespigny2007602-82">[82]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following Huan\'s death, Dou Wu and the Grand Tutor Chen Fan (<span lang="zh-Hant" title="Chinese language text">&#38515;&#34115;</span>) (d.&#160;168 AD) attempted a <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d\'&#233;tat">coup d\'&#233;tat</a> against the eunuchs <a href="/wiki/Hou_Lan" title="Hou Lan">Hou Lan</a> (d.&#160;172 AD), <a href="/wiki/Cao_Jie_(eunuch)" title="Cao Jie (eunuch)">Cao Jie</a> (d.&#160;181 AD), and Wang Fu (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#29579;&#29995;</span>). When the plot was uncovered, the eunuchs arrested <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empress_Dou_Miao" title="Empress Dou Miao">Empress Dowager Dou</a> (d.&#160;172&#160;AD) and Chen Fan. General Zhang Huan (<span lang="zh-Hant" title="Chinese language text">&#24373;&#22864;</span>) favored the eunuchs. He and his troops confronted Dou Wu and his retainers at the palace gate where each side shouted accusations of treason against the other. When the retainers gradually deserted Dou Wu, he was forced to commit suicide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeck1986319&#8211;322_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeck1986319&#8211;322-83">[83]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Under <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Ling_of_Han" title="Emperor Ling of Han">Emperor Ling</a> (r.&#160;168&#8211;189 AD) the eunuchs had the partisan prohibitions renewed and expanded, while also auctioning off top government offices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Many affairs of state were entrusted to the eunuchs <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhao_Zhong" title="Zhao Zhong">Zhao Zhong</a> (d.&#160;189 AD) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhang_Rang" title="Zhang Rang">Zhang Rang</a> (d.&#160;189 AD) while Emperor Ling spent much of his time <a href="/wiki/Sexual_roleplay" title="Sexual roleplay">roleplaying</a> with concubines and participating in military parades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Crespigny2007513&#8211;514_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Crespigny2007513&#8211;514-85">[85]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_the_Han_dynasty">End of the Han dynasty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: End of the Han dynasty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/End_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="End of the Han dynasty">End of the Han dynasty</a></div>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:408px;max-width:408px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Warring_States_or_Western_Han_crossbow.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="298" data-file-width="416" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Warring_States_or_Western_Han_crossbow.jpg/200px-Warring_States_or_Western_Han_crossbow.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Warring_States_or_Western_Han_crossbow.jpg/300px-Warring_States_or_Western_Han_crossbow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Warring_States_or_Western_Han_crossbow.jpg/400px-Warring_States_or_Western_Han_crossbow.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Warring_States_or_Western_Han_crossbow2.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="324" data-file-width="432" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Warring_States_or_Western_Han_crossbow2.jpg/200px-Warring_States_or_Western_Han_crossbow2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Warring_States_or_Western_Han_crossbow2.jpg/300px-Warring_States_or_Western_Han_crossbow2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Warring_States_or_Western_Han_crossbow2.jpg/400px-Warring_States_or_Western_Han_crossbow2.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">A Chinese <a href="/wiki/Crossbow" title="Crossbow">crossbow</a> mechanism with a buttplate from either the late Warring States Period or the early Han dynasty; made of bronze and inlaid with silver</div></div></div>\n<p>The Partisan Prohibitions were repealed during the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Turban_Rebellion" title="Yellow Turban Rebellion">Yellow Turban Rebellion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Way_of_the_Five_Pecks_of_Rice" title="Way of the Five Pecks of Rice">Five Pecks of Rice Rebellion</a> in 184 AD, largely because the court did not want to continue to alienate a significant portion of the <a href="/wiki/Four_occupations" title="Four occupations">gentry class</a> who might otherwise join the rebellions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Crespigny2007511_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Crespigny2007511-86">[86]</a></sup> The Yellow Turbans and Five-Pecks-of-Rice adherents belonged to two different hierarchical <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Daoist</a> religious societies led by <a href="/wiki/Faith_healing" title="Faith healing">faith healers</a> <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Jue" title="Zhang Jue">Zhang Jue</a> (d.&#160;184 AD) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zhang_Lu_(Three_Kingdoms)" title="Zhang Lu (Three Kingdoms)">Zhang Lu</a> (d.&#160;216 AD), respectively.
