Zhang Lu\'s rebellion, in modern northern <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan</a> and southern <a href="/wiki/Shaanxi" title="Shaanxi">Shaanxi</a>, was not quelled until 215 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1986628&#8211;629_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1986628&#8211;629-87">[87]</a></sup> Zhang Jue\'s massive rebellion across eight <a href="/wiki/Government_of_the_Han_dynasty#Provincial_authorities" title="Government of the Han dynasty">provinces</a> was annihilated by Han forces within a year, however the following decades saw much smaller recurrent uprisings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeck1986339&#8211;340_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeck1986339&#8211;340-88">[88]</a></sup> Although the Yellow Turbans were defeated, many generals appointed during the crisis never disbanded their assembled <a href="/wiki/Militia" title="Militia">militia</a> forces and used these troops to amass power outside of the collapsing imperial authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey199984_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey199984-89">[89]</a></sup>\n</p><p>General-in-Chief <a href="/wiki/He_Jin" title="He Jin">He Jin</a> (d.&#160;189 AD), half-brother to <a href="/wiki/Empress_He_(Han_dynasty)" title="Empress He (Han dynasty)">Empress He</a> (d. 189 AD), plotted with <a href="/wiki/Yuan_Shao" title="Yuan Shao">Yuan Shao</a> (d.&#160;202 AD) to overthrow the eunuchs by having several generals march to the outskirts of the capital. There, in a written petition to Empress He, they demanded the eunuchs\' execution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeck1986339&#8211;344_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeck1986339&#8211;344-90">[90]</a></sup> After a period of hesitation, Empress He consented. When the eunuchs discovered this, however, they had her brother He Miao (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#20309;&#33495;</span>) rescind the order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> The eunuchs assassinated He Jin on September 22, 189 AD. Yuan Shao then besieged Luoyang\'s Northern Palace while his brother <a href="/wiki/Yuan_Shu" title="Yuan Shu">Yuan Shu</a> (d.&#160;199 AD) besieged the Southern Palace. On September 25 both palaces were breached and approximately two thousand eunuchs were killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Zhang Rang had previously fled with <a href="/wiki/Liu_Bian" title="Liu Bian">Emperor Shao</a> (r.&#160;189 AD) and his brother Liu Xie&#8212;the future <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Xian_of_Han" title="Emperor Xian of Han">Emperor Xian of Han</a> (r. 189&#8211;220 AD). While being pursued by the Yuan brothers, Zhang committed suicide by jumping into the Yellow River.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeck1986345_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeck1986345-93">[93]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:443px;max-width:443px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Guardians_of_Day_and_Night,_Han_Dynasty.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1140" data-file-width="1208" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Guardians_of_Day_and_Night%2C_Han_Dynasty.jpg/180px-Guardians_of_Day_and_Night%2C_Han_Dynasty.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Guardians_of_Day_and_Night%2C_Han_Dynasty.jpg/270px-Guardians_of_Day_and_Night%2C_Han_Dynasty.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Guardians_of_Day_and_Night%2C_Han_Dynasty.jpg/360px-Guardians_of_Day_and_Night%2C_Han_Dynasty.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:257px;max-width:257px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dahuting_tomb_mural_detail_of_women_wearing_hanfu,_Eastern_Han_period.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="664" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Dahuting_tomb_mural_detail_of_women_wearing_hanfu%2C_Eastern_Han_period.jpg/255px-Dahuting_tomb_mural_detail_of_women_wearing_hanfu%2C_Eastern_Han_period.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Dahuting_tomb_mural_detail_of_women_wearing_hanfu%2C_Eastern_Han_period.jpg/383px-Dahuting_tomb_mural_detail_of_women_wearing_hanfu%2C_Eastern_Han_period.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Dahuting_tomb_mural_detail_of_women_wearing_hanfu%2C_Eastern_Han_period.jpg/510px-Dahuting_tomb_mural_detail_of_women_wearing_hanfu%2C_Eastern_Han_period.jpg 2x" width="255"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><b>Left:</b> Animalistic guardian spirits of day and night wearing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Chinese_clothing" title="Han Chinese clothing">Chinese robes</a>, Han dynasty <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_paintings" title="Chinese paintings">paintings</a> on ceramic <a href="/wiki/Tile" title="Tile">tile</a>; <a href="/wiki/Michael_Loewe" title="Michael Loewe">Michael Loewe</a> writes that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hybrid_(mythology)" title="Hybrid (mythology)">hybrid of man and beast</a> in art and religious beliefs predated the Han and remained popular during the first half of Western Han and the Eastern Han.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELoewe199438&#8211;52_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoewe199438&#8211;52-94">[94]</a></sup> <br/><b>Right:</b> Detail of a mural showing two women wearing <i><a href="/wiki/Hanfu" title="Hanfu">Hanfu</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a> robes, <a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Category:Dahuting_Tomb_murals" title="c:Category:Dahuting Tomb murals">from the Dahuting Tomb</a> (<span lang="zh-Hans">&#25171;&#34382;&#20141;&#27721;&#22675;</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">D&#225;h&#468;t&#237;ng h&#224;nm&#249;</span></i>) of the late <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="History of the Han dynasty">Eastern Han Dynasty</a> (25&#8211;220 AD), located in <a href="/wiki/Zhengzhou" title="Zhengzhou">Zhengzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henan" title="Henan">Henan</a></div></div></div>\n<p>General <a href="/wiki/Dong_Zhuo" title="Dong Zhuo">Dong Zhuo</a> (d.&#160;192 AD) found the young emperor and his brother wandering in the countryside. He escorted them safely back to the capital and was made <a href="/wiki/Government_of_the_Han_dynasty#Minister_of_Works" title="Government of the Han dynasty">Minister of Works</a>, taking control of Luoyang and forcing Yuan Shao to flee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeck1986345&#8211;346_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeck1986345&#8211;346-95">[95]</a></sup> After Dong Zhuo demoted Emperor Shao and promoted his brother Liu Xie as Emperor Xian, Yuan Shao led a coalition of former officials and officers against Dong, who burned Luoyang to the ground and resettled the court at Chang\'an in May 191 AD. Dong Zhuo later poisoned Emperor Shao.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeck1986346&#8211;349_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeck1986346&#8211;349-96">[96]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dong was killed by his adopted son <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BC_Bu" title="L&#252; Bu">L&#252; Bu</a> (d.&#160;198 AD) in a plot hatched by <a href="/wiki/Wang_Yun_(Han_dynasty)" title="Wang Yun (Han dynasty)">Wang Yun</a> (d. 192 AD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Crespigny2007158_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Crespigny2007158-97">[97]</a></sup> Emperor Xian fled from Chang\'an in 195 AD to the ruins of Luoyang. Xian was persuaded by <a href="/wiki/Cao_Cao" title="Cao Cao">Cao Cao</a> (155&#8211;220 AD), then Governor of Yan Province in modern western <a href="/wiki/Shandong" title="Shandong">Shandong</a> and eastern <a href="/wiki/Henan" title="Henan">Henan</a>, to move the capital to <a href="/wiki/Xuchang" title="Xuchang">Xuchang</a> in 196 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Yuan Shao challenged Cao Cao for control over the emperor. Yuan\'s power was greatly diminished after Cao defeated him at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Guandu" title="Battle of Guandu">Battle of Guandu</a> in 200 AD. After Yuan died, Cao killed Yuan Shao\'s son <a href="/wiki/Yuan_Tan" title="Yuan Tan">Yuan Tan</a> (173&#8211;205 AD), who had fought with his brothers over the family inheritance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beck_1986_351_352_crespigny_2007_36_37_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-beck_1986_351_352_crespigny_2007_36_37-99">[99]</a></sup> His brothers <a href="/wiki/Yuan_Shang" title="Yuan Shang">Yuan Shang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yuan_Xi" title="Yuan Xi">Yuan Xi</a> were killed in 207 AD by <a href="/wiki/Gongsun_Kang" title="Gongsun Kang">Gongsun Kang</a> (d.&#160;221 AD), who sent their heads to Cao Cao.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beck_1986_351_352_crespigny_2007_36_37_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-beck_1986_351_352_crespigny_2007_36_37-99">[99]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After Cao\'s defeat at the naval <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Red_Cliffs" title="Battle of Red Cliffs">Battle of Red Cliffs</a> in 208 AD, China was divided into three spheres of influence, with Cao Cao dominating the north, <a href="/wiki/Sun_Quan" title="Sun Quan">Sun Quan</a> (182&#8211;252 AD) dominating the south, and <a href="/wiki/Liu_Bei" title="Liu Bei">Liu Bei</a> (161&#8211;223 AD) dominating the west.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Cao Cao died in March 220 AD. By December his son <a href="/wiki/Cao_Pi" title="Cao Pi">Cao Pi</a> (187&#8211;226 AD) had Emperor Xian relinquish the throne to him and is known posthumously as <a href="/wiki/Cao_Pi" title="Cao Pi">Emperor Wen of Wei</a>. This formally ended the Han dynasty and initiated an age of conflict between <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms" title="Three Kingdoms">three states</a>: <a href="/wiki/Cao_Wei" title="Cao Wei">Cao Wei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Wu" title="Eastern Wu">Eastern Wu</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Shu_Han" title="Shu Han">Shu Han</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Society and culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Society_and_culture_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="Society and culture of the Han dynasty">Society and culture of the Han dynasty</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_class">Social class</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Social class">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_nobility" title="Chinese nobility">Chinese nobility</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duke_Yansheng#Qin_dynasty_.28221.E2.80.93206_BCE.29_and_the_Western_and_Eastern_Han_dynasties_.28206_BCE_.E2.80.93_220_CE.29" title="Duke Yansheng">Marquis Baocheng</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Four_occupations" title="Four occupations">Four occupations</a></div>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:308px;max-width:308px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:137px;max-width:137px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lacquerware_bowl,_Western_Han_Dynasty.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="101" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Lacquerware_bowl%2C_Western_Han_Dynasty.JPG/135px-Lacquerware_bowl%2C_Western_Han_Dynasty.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Lacquerware_bowl%2C_Western_Han_Dynasty.JPG/203px-Lacquerware_bowl%2C_Western_Han_Dynasty.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Lacquerware_bowl%2C_Western_Han_Dynasty.JPG/270px-Lacquerware_bowl%2C_Western_Han_Dynasty.JPG 2x" width="135"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:167px;max-width:167px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CapitalMuseum15.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="972" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/CapitalMuseum15.jpg/165px-CapitalMuseum15.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/CapitalMuseum15.jpg/248px-CapitalMuseum15.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/CapitalMuseum15.jpg/330px-CapitalMuseum15.jpg 2x" width="165"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Two Han-dynasty red-and-black <a href="/wiki/Lacquerware" title="Lacquerware">lacquerwares</a>, one a bowl, the other a tray; usually only wealthy officials, nobles, and merchants could afford domestic luxury items like lacquerwares, which were common commodities produced by skilled artisans and craftsmen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:358px;max-width:358px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mural_Painting_of_a_Banquet_Scene_from_the_Han_Dynasty_Tomb_of_Ta-hu-t%27ing.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="664" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Mural_Painting_of_a_Banquet_Scene_from_the_Han_Dynasty_Tomb_of_Ta-hu-t%27ing.jpg/220px-Mural_Painting_of_a_Banquet_Scene_from_the_Han_Dynasty_Tomb_of_Ta-hu-t%27ing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Mural_Painting_of_a_Banquet_Scene_from_the_Han_Dynasty_Tomb_of_Ta-hu-t%27ing.jpg/330px-Mural_Painting_of_a_Banquet_Scene_from_the_Han_Dynasty_Tomb_of_Ta-hu-t%27ing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Mural_Painting_of_a_Banquet_Scene_from_the_Han_Dynasty_Tomb_of_Ta-hu-t%27ing.jpg/440px-Mural_Painting_of_a_Banquet_Scene_from_the_Han_Dynasty_Tomb_of_Ta-hu-t%27ing.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:132px;max-width:132px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eastern_Han_Luoyang_Mural_of_Liubo_players.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="701" data-file-width="619" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Eastern_Han_Luoyang_Mural_of_Liubo_players.jpg/130px-Eastern_Han_Luoyang_Mural_of_Liubo_players.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Eastern_Han_Luoyang_Mural_of_Liubo_players.jpg/195px-Eastern_Han_Luoyang_Mural_of_Liubo_players.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Eastern_Han_Luoyang_Mural_of_Liubo_players.jpg/260px-Eastern_Han_Luoyang_Mural_of_Liubo_players.jpg 2x" width="130"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><b>Left:</b> a late <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="History of the Han dynasty">Eastern Han</a> (25&#8211;220 AD) <a href="/wiki/Chinese_painting" title="Chinese painting">Chinese tomb mural</a> showing lively scenes of a banquet (yanyin <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#23476;&#39154;</span>), dance and music (wuyue <span lang="zh-Hant" title="Chinese language text">&#33310;&#27138;</span>), acrobatics (baixi <span lang="zh-Hant" title="Chinese language text">&#30334;&#25138;</span>), and wrestling (xiangbu <span lang="zh-Hant" title="Chinese language text">&#30456;&#25778;</span>), <a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Category:Dahuting_Tomb_murals" title="c:Category:Dahuting Tomb murals">from the Dahuting Tomb</a> (<span lang="zh-Hant">&#25171;&#34382;&#20141;&#28450;&#22675;</span>; <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">D&#225;h&#468;t&#237;ng h&#224;nm&#249;</span></i>), on the southern bank of the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Siuhe_River&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Siuhe River (page does not exist)">Siuhe River</a> in <a href="/wiki/Zhengzhou" title="Zhengzhou">Zhengzhou</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henan_province" title="Henan province">Henan province</a> (just west of <a href="/wiki/Xi_County,_Henan" title="Xi County, Henan">Xi County</a>)<br/><b>Right:</b> a mural from an <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="History of the Han dynasty">Eastern Han</a> tomb at Zhucun (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#26417;&#26449;</span>), <a href="/wiki/Luoyang" title="Luoyang">Luoyang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henan" title="Henan">Henan</a> province; the two figures in the foreground are playing <a href="/wiki/Liubo" title="Liubo">liubo</a>, with the playing mat between them, and the liubo game board to the side of the mat.</div></div></div>\n<p>In the hierarchical social order, the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_China" title="Emperor of China">emperor</a> was at the apex of Han society and government. However the emperor was often a minor, ruled over by a <a href="/wiki/Regent" title="Regent">regent</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Empress_dowager" title="Empress dowager">empress dowager</a> or one of her male relatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;197266&#8211;72_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;197266&#8211;72-103">[103]</a></sup> Ranked immediately below the emperor were <a href="/wiki/Kings_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="Kings of the Han dynasty">the kings</a> who were of the same <a href="/wiki/Liu" title="Liu">Liu</a> family clan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> The rest of society, including <a href="/wiki/Chinese_nobility" title="Chinese nobility">nobles</a> lower than kings and all commoners excluding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave" title="Slave">slaves</a> belonged to one of twenty ranks (<i>ershi gongcheng</i> <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#20108;&#21313;&#20844;&#20056;</span>).\n</p><p>Each successive rank gave its holder greater pensions and legal privileges. The highest rank, of full <a href="/wiki/Marquess" title="Marquess">marquess</a>, came with a state pension and a territorial <a href="/wiki/Fief" title="Fief">fiefdom</a>. Holders of the rank immediately below, that of ordinary marquess, received a pension, but had no territorial rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scholar-bureaucrat" title="Scholar-bureaucrat">Officials</a> who served in government belonged to the wider commoner social class and were ranked just below nobles in social prestige. The highest government officials could be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enfeoffment" title="Enfeoffment">enfeoffed</a> as marquesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;197284_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;197284-106">[106]</a></sup> By the Eastern Han period, local elites of unattached scholars, teachers, students, and government officials began to identify themselves as members of a larger, nationwide <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gentry_(China)" title="Gentry (China)">gentry class</a> with shared values and a commitment to mainstream scholarship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> When the government became noticeably corrupt in mid-to-late Eastern Han, many gentrymen even considered the cultivation of morally grounded personal relationships more important than serving in public office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Farmer" title="Farmer">farmer</a>, or specifically the small landowner-cultivator, was ranked just below scholars and officials in the social hierarchy. Other agricultural cultivators were of a lower status, such as <a href="/wiki/Leasehold_estate" title="Leasehold estate">tenants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Day_labor" title="Day labor">wage laborers</a>, and in rare cases slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Artisan" title="Artisan">Artisans</a>, technicians, tradespeople and craftsmen had a legal and <a href="/wiki/Socioeconomic_status" title="Socioeconomic status">socioeconomic status</a> between that of owner-cultivator farmers and common <a href="/wiki/Merchant" title="Merchant">merchants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;1972112_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;1972112-110">[110]</a></sup> State-registered merchants, who were forced by law to wear white-colored clothes and pay high commercial taxes, were considered by the gentry as social parasites with a contemptible status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> These were often petty shopkeepers of urban marketplaces; merchants such as industrialists and itinerant traders working between a network of cities could avoid registering as merchants and were often wealthier and more powerful than the vast majority of government officials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Wealthy landowners, such as nobles and officials, often provided lodging for retainers who provided valuable work or duties, sometimes including fighting bandits or riding into battle. Unlike slaves, retainers could come and go from their master\'s home as they pleased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;1972127&#8211;128_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;1972127&#8211;128-113">[113]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">Medical physicians</a>, pig breeders, and <a href="/wiki/Butcher" title="Butcher">butchers</a> had a fairly high social status, while <a href="/wiki/Divination" title="Divination">occultist diviners</a>, runners, and messengers had low status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Marriage.2C_gender.2C_and_kinship"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage,_gender,_and_kinship">Marriage, gender, and kinship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Marriage, gender, and kinship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Women_in_ancient_and_imperial_China#Han_dynasty" title="Women in ancient and imperial China">Women in Han China</a></div>\n<p>The Han-era family was <a href="/wiki/Patrilineality" title="Patrilineality">patrilineal</a> and typically had four to five <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_family" title="Nuclear family">nuclear family</a> members living in one household. Multiple generations of <a href="/wiki/Extended_family" title="Extended family">extended family</a> members did not occupy the same house, unlike families of later dynasties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucian family norms</a>, various family members were treated with different levels of respect and intimacy. For example, there were different accepted time frames for mourning the death of a father versus a paternal uncle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;19729&#8211;10_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;19729&#8211;10-116">[116]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Marriages were highly ritualized, particularly for the wealthy, and included many important steps. The giving of betrothal gifts, known as <a href="/wiki/Bride_price" title="Bride price">bridewealth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dowry" title="Dowry">dowry</a>, were especially important. A lack of either was considered dishonorable and the woman would have been seen not as a wife, but as a <a href="/wiki/Concubinage" title="Concubinage">concubine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiesner-Hanks201130_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiesner-Hanks201130-117">[117]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arranged_marriage" title="Arranged marriage">Arranged marriages</a> were normal, with the father\'s input on his offspring\'s spouse being considered more important than the mother\'s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Monogamy" title="Monogamy">Monogamous marriages</a> were also normal, although nobles and high officials were wealthy enough to afford and support concubines as additional lovers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Under certain conditions dictated by custom, not law, both men and women were able to <a href="/wiki/Divorce" title="Divorce">divorce</a> their spouses and remarry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> However, a woman who had been widowed continued to belong to her husband\'s family after his death. In order to remarry, the widow would have to be returned to her family in exchange for a ransom fee. Her children would not be allowed to go with her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWiesner-Hanks201130_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWiesner-Hanks201130-117">[117]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:308px;max-width:308px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:177px;max-width:177px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_069.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="263" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_069.jpg/175px-Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_069.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_069.jpg/263px-Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_069.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_069.jpg/350px-Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_069.jpg 2x" width="175"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:127px;max-width:127px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nswag,_dinastia_han,_figurina_dipinta_di_danzatrice.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1747" data-file-width="834" decoding="async" height="262" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Nswag%2C_dinastia_han%2C_figurina_dipinta_di_danzatrice.jpg/125px-Nswag%2C_dinastia_han%2C_figurina_dipinta_di_danzatrice.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Nswag%2C_dinastia_han%2C_figurina_dipinta_di_danzatrice.jpg/188px-Nswag%2C_dinastia_han%2C_figurina_dipinta_di_danzatrice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Nswag%2C_dinastia_han%2C_figurina_dipinta_di_danzatrice.jpg/250px-Nswag%2C_dinastia_han%2C_figurina_dipinta_di_danzatrice.jpg 2x" width="125"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><b>Left image</b>: A <a href="/wiki/Chinese_ceramics" title="Chinese ceramics">Han pottery</a> female servant in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Chinese_clothing" title="Han Chinese clothing">silk robes</a><br/> <b>Right image</b>: A Han pottery female dancer in silk robes</div></div></div>\n<p>Apart from the passing of noble titles or ranks, <a href="/wiki/Inheritance" title="Inheritance">inheritance</a> practices did not involve <a href="/wiki/Primogeniture" title="Primogeniture">primogeniture</a>; each son received an equal share of the family property.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;197216&#8211;17_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;197216&#8211;17-121">[121]</a></sup> Unlike the practice in later dynasties, the father usually sent his adult married sons away with their portions of the family fortune.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;19726&#8211;9_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;19726&#8211;9-122">[122]</a></sup> Daughters received a portion of the family fortune through their <a href="/wiki/Dowry" title="Dowry">marriage dowries</a>, though this was usually much less than the shares of sons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;197217&#8211;18_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;197217&#8211;18-123">[123]</a></sup> A different distribution of the remainder could be specified in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Last_will_and_testament" title="Last will and testament">will</a>, but it is unclear how common this was.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;197217_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;197217-124">[124]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Women were expected to obey the will of their father, then their husband, and then their adult son in old age. However, it is known from contemporary sources that there were many deviations to this rule, especially in regard to mothers over their sons, and empresses who ordered around and openly humiliated their fathers and brothers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;197249&#8211;59_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;197249&#8211;59-125">[125]</a></sup> Women were exempt from the annual <a href="/wiki/Corv%C3%A9e" title="Corv&#233;e">corv&#233;e</a> labor duties, but often engaged in a range of income-earning occupations aside from their domestic chores of cooking and cleaning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHinsch200274&#8211;75_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHinsch200274&#8211;75-126">[126]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The most common occupation for women was weaving clothes for the family, sale at market or for large textile enterprises that employed hundreds of women. Other women helped on their brothers\' farms or became singers, dancers, <a href="/wiki/Witchcraft" title="Witchcraft">sorceresses</a>, respected medical physicians, and successful merchants who could afford their own silk clothes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> Some women formed spinning collectives, aggregating the resources of several different families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHinsch200229_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHinsch200229-128">[128]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Education.2C_literature.2C_and_philosophy"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Education,_literature,_and_philosophy">Education, literature, and philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Education, literature, and philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:308px;max-width:308px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Holding_Shield_and_Holding_Broom_tomb_doors,_1_of_2,_China,_unearthed_from_Lanjia_Yard,_Pixian_County,_Sichuan,_Eastern_Han_dynasty,_25-220_AD,_stone_-_Sichuan_Provincial_Museum_-_Chengdu,_China_-_DSC04745.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="5472" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Holding_Shield_and_Holding_Broom_tomb_doors%2C_1_of_2%2C_China%2C_unearthed_from_Lanjia_Yard%2C_Pixian_County%2C_Sichuan%2C_Eastern_Han_dynasty%2C_25-220_AD%2C_stone_-_Sichuan_Provincial_Museum_-_Chengdu%2C_China_-_DSC04745.jpg/150px-thumbnail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Holding_Shield_and_Holding_Broom_tomb_doors%2C_1_of_2%2C_China%2C_unearthed_from_Lanjia_Yard%2C_Pixian_County%2C_Sichuan%2C_Eastern_Han_dynasty%2C_25-220_AD%2C_stone_-_Sichuan_Provincial_Museum_-_Chengdu%2C_China_-_DSC04745.jpg/225px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Holding_Shield_and_Holding_Broom_tomb_doors%2C_1_of_2%2C_China%2C_unearthed_from_Lanjia_Yard%2C_Pixian_County%2C_Sichuan%2C_Eastern_Han_dynasty%2C_25-220_AD%2C_stone_-_Sichuan_Provincial_Museum_-_Chengdu%2C_China_-_DSC04745.jpg/300px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Holding_Shield_and_Holding_Broom_tomb_doors,_2_of_2,_China,_unearthed_from_Lanjia_Yard,_Pixian_County,_Sichuan,_Eastern_Han_dynasty,_25-220_AD,_stone_-_Sichuan_Provincial_Museum_-_Chengdu,_China_-_DSC04747.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="5281" data-file-width="3457" decoding="async" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Holding_Shield_and_Holding_Broom_tomb_doors%2C_2_of_2%2C_China%2C_unearthed_from_Lanjia_Yard%2C_Pixian_County%2C_Sichuan%2C_Eastern_Han_dynasty%2C_25-220_AD%2C_stone_-_Sichuan_Provincial_Museum_-_Chengdu%2C_China_-_DSC04747.jpg/150px-thumbnail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Holding_Shield_and_Holding_Broom_tomb_doors%2C_2_of_2%2C_China%2C_unearthed_from_Lanjia_Yard%2C_Pixian_County%2C_Sichuan%2C_Eastern_Han_dynasty%2C_25-220_AD%2C_stone_-_Sichuan_Provincial_Museum_-_Chengdu%2C_China_-_DSC04747.jpg/225px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Holding_Shield_and_Holding_Broom_tomb_doors%2C_2_of_2%2C_China%2C_unearthed_from_Lanjia_Yard%2C_Pixian_County%2C_Sichuan%2C_Eastern_Han_dynasty%2C_25-220_AD%2C_stone_-_Sichuan_Provincial_Museum_-_Chengdu%2C_China_-_DSC04747.jpg/300px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Carved <a href="/wiki/Relief" title="Relief">reliefs</a> on stone tomb doors showing men dressed in <i><a href="/wiki/Hanfu" title="Hanfu">Hanfu</a></i>, with one holding a shield, the other a broom, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Han_Dynasty" title="Eastern Han Dynasty">Eastern Han Dynasty</a> (25&#8211;220 AD), from Lanjia Yard, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pi_County" title="Pi County">Pi County</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sichuan_province" title="Sichuan province">Sichuan province</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sichuan_Provincial_Museum&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sichuan Provincial Museum (page does not exist)">Sichuan Provincial Museum</a> of <a href="/wiki/Chengdu" title="Chengdu">Chengdu</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The early Western Han court simultaneously accepted the philosophical teachings of <a href="/wiki/Legalism_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Legalism (Chinese philosophy)">Legalism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huang-Lao" title="Huang-Lao">Huang-Lao Daoism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a> in making state decisions and shaping government policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> However, the Han court under <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wu_of_Han" title="Emperor Wu of Han">Emperor Wu</a> gave Confucianism exclusive patronage. He abolished all academic chairs or erudites (<i>b&#243;sh&#236;</i> &#21338;&#22763;) not dealing with the Confucian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_Classics" title="Five Classics">Five Classics</a> in 136 BC and encouraged nominees for office to receive a Confucian-based education at the <a href="/wiki/Taixue" title="Taixue">Imperial University</a> that he established in 124 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Unlike the original ideology espoused by <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a>, or Kongzi (551&#8211;479 BC), Han Confucianism in Emperor Wu\'s reign was the creation of <a href="/wiki/Dong_Zhongshu" title="Dong Zhongshu">Dong Zhongshu</a> (179&#8211;104 BC). Dong was a scholar and minor official who aggregated the ethical Confucian ideas of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Li_(Confucian)" title="Li (Confucian)">ritual</a>, <a href="/wiki/Filial_piety" title="Filial piety">filial piety</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ren_(Confucianism)" title="Ren (Confucianism)">harmonious relationships</a> with <a href="/wiki/Wu_Xing" title="Wu Xing">five phases</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yin_and_yang" title="Yin and yang">yin-yang</a> cosmologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> Much to the interest of the ruler, Dong\'s synthesis justified the imperial system of government within the natural order of the universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKramers1986754_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKramers1986754-132">[132]</a></sup> The Imperial University grew in importance as the student body grew to over 30,000 by the 2nd century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> A Confucian-based education was also made available at commandery-level schools and <a href="/wiki/Private_school" title="Private school">private schools</a> opened in small towns, where teachers earned respectable incomes from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuition" title="Tuition">tuition</a> payments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;1972103_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;1972103-134">[134]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_fragment_of_Xiping_stone_classics.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3329" data-file-width="2219" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_fragment_of_Xiping_stone_classics.jpg/170px-CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_fragment_of_Xiping_stone_classics.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_fragment_of_Xiping_stone_classics.jpg/255px-CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_fragment_of_Xiping_stone_classics.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_fragment_of_Xiping_stone_classics.jpg/340px-CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_fragment_of_Xiping_stone_classics.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CMOC_Treasures_of_Ancient_China_exhibit_-_fragment_of_Xiping_stone_classics.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A fragment of the \'Stone Classics\' (&#29113;&#24179;&#30707;&#32147;); these stone-carved <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_Classics" title="Five Classics">Five Classics</a> installed during <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Ling_of_Han" title="Emperor Ling of Han">Emperor Ling\'s</a> reign along the roadside of the <a href="/wiki/Taixue" title="Taixue">Imperial University</a> (right outside <a href="/wiki/Luoyang" title="Luoyang">Luoyang</a>) were made at the instigation of <a href="/wiki/Cai_Yong" title="Cai Yong">Cai Yong</a> (132&#8211;192 AD), who feared the Classics housed in the imperial library were being <a href="/wiki/Interpolation_(manuscripts)" title="Interpolation (manuscripts)">interpolated</a> by University Academicians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Some important texts were created and studied by scholars. Philosophical works written by <a href="/wiki/Yang_Xiong_(author)" title="Yang Xiong (author)">Yang Xiong</a> (53 BC&#8211;18 AD), <a href="/wiki/Huan_Tan" title="Huan Tan">Huan Tan</a> (43 BC&#8211;28 AD), <a href="/wiki/Wang_Chong" title="Wang Chong">Wang Chong</a> (27&#8211;100 AD), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wang_Fu_(philosopher)" title="Wang Fu (philosopher)">Wang Fu</a> (78&#8211;163 AD) questioned whether human nature was innately good or evil and posed challenges to Dong\'s universal order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECh\'en1986773&#8211;794_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECh\'en1986773&#8211;794-136">[136]</a></sup> The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shiji" title="Shiji">Records of the Grand Historian</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Sima_Tan" title="Sima Tan">Sima Tan</a> (d. 110 BC) and his son <a href="/wiki/Sima_Qian" title="Sima Qian">Sima Qian</a> (145&#8211;86 BC) <a href="/wiki/Chinese_historiography" title="Chinese historiography">established the standard model</a> for all of imperial China\'s <a href="/wiki/Twenty-Four_Histories" title="Twenty-Four Histories">Standard Histories</a>, such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Han" title="Book of Han">Book of Han</a></i> written by <a href="/wiki/Ban_Biao" title="Ban Biao">Ban Biao</a> (3&#8211;54 AD), his son <a href="/wiki/Ban_Gu" title="Ban Gu">Ban Gu</a> (32&#8211;92 AD), and his daughter <a href="/wiki/Ban_Zhao" title="Ban Zhao">Ban Zhao</a> (45&#8211;116 AD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> There were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_dictionaries" title="Chinese dictionaries">dictionaries</a> such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Shuowen_Jiezi" title="Shuowen Jiezi">Shuowen Jiezi</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Xu_Shen" title="Xu Shen">Xu Shen</a> (c. 58&#8211;c. 147 AD) and the <i><a href="/wiki/Fangyan" title="Fangyan">Fangyan</a></i> by Yang Xiong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Biography" title="Biography">Biographies</a> on important figures were written by various gentrymen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey1986645_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey1986645-139">[139]</a></sup> Han dynasty poetry was dominated by the <a href="/wiki/Fu_(poetry)" title="Fu (poetry)"><i>fu</i> genre</a>, which achieved its greatest prominence during the reign of Emperor Wu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law_and_order">Law and order</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Law and order">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Han scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Jia_Yi" title="Jia Yi">Jia Yi</a> (201&#8211;169 BC) portrayed the previous <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin dynasty</a> as a brutal regime. However, archaeological evidence from <a href="/wiki/Zhangjiashan_Han_bamboo_texts" title="Zhangjiashan Han bamboo texts">Zhangjiashan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shuihudi_Qin_bamboo_texts" title="Shuihudi Qin bamboo texts">Shuihudi</a> reveal that many of the <a href="/wiki/Statute" title="Statute">statutes</a> in the Han <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Code_(law)" title="Code (law)">law code</a> compiled by Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Xiao_He" title="Xiao He">Xiao He</a> (d.&#160;193 BC) were derived from Qin law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Various cases for <a href="/wiki/Rape" title="Rape">rape</a>, physical abuse and <a href="/wiki/Murder" title="Murder">murder</a> were prosecuted in court. Women, although usually having fewer rights by custom, were allowed to level civil and criminal charges against men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> While suspects were jailed, convicted criminals were never imprisoned. Instead, punishments were commonly monetary fines, periods of forced hard labor for convicts, and the penalty of death by beheading.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHulsew&#233;1986532&#8211;535_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHulsew&#233;1986532&#8211;535-143">[143]</a></sup> Early Han punishments of <a href="/wiki/Torture" title="Torture">torturous</a> <a href="/wiki/Mutilation" title="Mutilation">mutilation</a> were borrowed from Qin law. A series of reforms abolished mutilation punishments with progressively less-severe beatings by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bastinado" title="Bastinado">bastinado</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHulsew&#233;1986531&#8211;533_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHulsew&#233;1986531&#8211;533-144">[144]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Acting as a judge in lawsuits was one of many duties of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/County_magistrate_(China)" title="County magistrate (China)">county magistrate</a> and Administrators of commanderies. Complex, high-profile or unresolved cases were often deferred to the Minister of Justice in the capital or even the emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHulsew&#233;1986528&#8211;529_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHulsew&#233;1986528&#8211;529-145">[145]</a></sup> In each Han county was several districts, each overseen by a <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_police" title="Chief of police">chief of police</a>. Order in the cities was maintained by government officers in the marketplaces and <a href="/wiki/Constable" title="Constable">constables</a> in the neighborhoods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Food">Food</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Food">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The most common staple crops consumed during Han were <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Foxtail_millet" title="Foxtail millet">foxtail millet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Proso_millet" title="Proso millet">proso millet</a>, rice, and <a href="/wiki/Bean" title="Bean">beans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWang198252_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWang198252-147">[147]</a></sup> Commonly eaten fruits and vegetables included chestnuts, pears, plums, peaches, melons, apricots, strawberries, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_bayberry" title="Red bayberry">red bayberries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jujube" title="Jujube">jujubes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calabash" title="Calabash">calabash</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bamboo_shoot" title="Bamboo shoot">bamboo shoots</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mustard_plant" title="Mustard plant">mustard plant</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taro" title="Taro">taro</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWang198253,_206_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWang198253,_206-148">[148]</a></sup> Domesticated animals that were also eaten included chickens, <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_duck" title="Mandarin duck">Mandarin ducks</a>, geese, cows, sheep, pigs, camels and dogs (various types were bred specifically for food, while most were used as pets). Turtles and fish were taken from streams and lakes. Commonly hunted game, such as owl, pheasant, magpie, <a href="/wiki/Sika_deer" title="Sika deer">sika deer</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_bamboo_partridge" title="Chinese bamboo partridge">Chinese bamboo partridge</a> were consumed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWang198257&#8211;58_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWang198257&#8211;58-149">[149]</a></sup> Seasonings included sugar, honey, salt and <a href="/wiki/Soy_sauce" title="Soy sauce">soy sauce</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHansen2000119&#8211;121_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHansen2000119&#8211;121-150">[150]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_alcoholic_beverages" title="Chinese alcoholic beverages">Beer and wine</a> were regularly consumed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Clothing">Clothing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Clothing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Hanfu" title="Hanfu">Hanfu</a></div>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:408px;max-width:408px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Silk_from_Mawangdui_2.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Silk_from_Mawangdui_2.jpg/200px-Silk_from_Mawangdui_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Silk_from_Mawangdui_2.jpg/300px-Silk_from_Mawangdui_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Silk_from_Mawangdui_2.jpg/400px-Silk_from_Mawangdui_2.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Silk_from_Mawangdui.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="778" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Silk_from_Mawangdui.jpg/200px-Silk_from_Mawangdui.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Silk_from_Mawangdui.jpg/300px-Silk_from_Mawangdui.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Silk_from_Mawangdui.jpg/400px-Silk_from_Mawangdui.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Woven <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a> textiles from Tomb No. 1 at <a href="/wiki/Mawangdui" title="Mawangdui">Mawangdui</a>, <a href="/wiki/Changsha" title="Changsha">Changsha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hunan" title="Hunan">Hunan</a> province, China, 2nd century BC</div></div></div>\n<p>The types of clothing worn and the materials used during the Han period depended upon social class. Wealthy folk could afford <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a> robes, skirts, socks, and mittens, coats made of <a href="/wiki/Fur_clothing" title="Fur clothing">badger or fox fur</a>, duck plumes, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slippers" title="Slippers">slippers</a> with inlaid leather, <a href="/wiki/Pearl" title="Pearl">pearls</a>, and silk lining. Peasants commonly wore clothes made of <a href="/wiki/Hemp" title="Hemp">hemp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">wool</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ferret" title="Ferret">ferret</a> skins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Religion.2C_cosmology.2C_and_metaphysics"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion,_cosmology,_and_metaphysics">Religion, cosmology, and metaphysics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Religion, cosmology, and metaphysics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bronze_Chimera,_Eastern_Han_Dynasty.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Bronze_Chimera%2C_Eastern_Han_Dynasty.jpg/220px-Bronze_Chimera%2C_Eastern_Han_Dynasty.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Bronze_Chimera%2C_Eastern_Han_Dynasty.jpg/330px-Bronze_Chimera%2C_Eastern_Han_Dynasty.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Bronze_Chimera%2C_Eastern_Han_Dynasty.jpg/440px-Bronze_Chimera%2C_Eastern_Han_Dynasty.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bronze_Chimera,_Eastern_Han_Dynasty.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An Eastern-Han bronze statuette of a <a href="/wiki/Qilin" title="Qilin">mythical chimera</a> (<i>qilin</i>), 1st century AD</div></div></div>\n<p>Families throughout Han China made ritual sacrifices of animals and food to deities, spirits, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancestor_worship" title="Ancestor worship">ancestors</a> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temple_(Chinese)" title="Temple (Chinese)">temples and shrines</a>. They believed that these items could be utilized by those in the spiritual realm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;197230&#8211;31_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;197230&#8211;31-153">[153]</a></sup> It was thought that each person had a <a href="/wiki/Hun_and_po" title="Hun and po">two-part soul</a>: the spirit-soul (<i>hun</i> &#39746;) which journeyed to the afterlife paradise of immortals (<i><a href="/wiki/Xian_(Taoism)" title="Xian (Taoism)">xian</a></i>), and the body-soul (<i>po</i> &#39748;) which remained in its grave or tomb on earth and was only reunited with the spirit-soul through a ritual ceremony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In addition to his many other roles, the emperor acted as the highest priest in the land who made sacrifices to <a href="/wiki/Tian" title="Tian">Heaven</a>, the main deities known as the <a href="/wiki/Three_Sovereigns_and_Five_Emperors" title="Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors">Five Powers</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Shen_(Chinese_religion)" title="Shen (Chinese religion)">spirits</a> (<i>shen</i> &#31070;) of mountains and rivers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;197271_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;197271-155">[155]</a></sup> It was believed that the three realms of Heaven, Earth, and Mankind were linked by natural cycles of <a href="/wiki/Yin_and_yang" title="Yin and yang">yin and yang</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Wu_Xing" title="Wu Xing">five phases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> If the emperor did not behave according to proper ritual, ethics, and morals, he could disrupt the fine balance of these cosmological cycles and cause calamities such as earthquakes, floods, droughts, epidemics, and swarms of locusts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rubbing_of_a_Han_Citang.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1603" data-file-width="2420" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Rubbing_of_a_Han_Citang.JPG/220px-Rubbing_of_a_Han_Citang.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Rubbing_of_a_Han_Citang.JPG/330px-Rubbing_of_a_Han_Citang.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Rubbing_of_a_Han_Citang.JPG/440px-Rubbing_of_a_Han_Citang.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rubbing_of_a_Han_Citang.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A rubbing of a Han pictorial stone showing an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancestor_worship" title="Ancestor worship">ancestral worship hall</a> (<i>citang</i> &#31072;&#22530;)</div></div></div>\n<p>It was believed that <a href="/wiki/Immortality" title="Immortality">immortality</a> could be achieved if one reached the lands of the <a href="/wiki/Queen_Mother_of_the_West" title="Queen Mother of the West">Queen Mother of the West</a> or <a href="/wiki/Mount_Penglai" title="Mount Penglai">Mount Penglai</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> Han-era <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daoism" title="Daoism">Daoists</a> assembled into small groups of hermits who attempted to achieve immortality through breathing exercises, sexual techniques and use of <a href="/wiki/Elixir_of_life" title="Elixir of life">medical elixirs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHansen2000144_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHansen2000144-159">[159]</a></sup> By the 2nd century AD, Daoists formed large hierarchical religious societies such as the <a href="/wiki/Way_of_the_Five_Pecks_of_Rice" title="Way of the Five Pecks of Rice">Way of the Five Pecks of Rice</a>. Its followers believed that the sage-philosopher <a href="/wiki/Laozi" title="Laozi">Laozi</a> (fl.&#160;6th century BC) was a holy <a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">prophet</a> who would offer <a href="/wiki/Salvation" title="Salvation">salvation</a> and good health if his devout followers would <a href="/wiki/Confession_(religion)" title="Confession (religion)">confess their sins</a>, ban the worship of unclean gods who accepted meat sacrifices and chant sections of the <i><a href="/wiki/Tao_Te_Ching" title="Tao Te Ching">Daodejing</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHansen2000144&#8211;146_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHansen2000144&#8211;146-160">[160]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> first entered China during the Eastern Han and was first mentioned in 65 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-needham_1972_112_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-needham_1972_112-161">[161]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Liu_Ying_(prince)" title="Liu Ying (prince)">Liu Ying</a> (d.&#160;71 AD), a half-brother to <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Ming_of_Han" title="Emperor Ming of Han">Emperor Ming of Han</a> (r.&#160;57&#8211;75 AD), was one of its earliest Chinese adherents, although <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Buddhism" title="Chinese Buddhism">Chinese Buddhism</a> at this point was heavily associated with <a href="/wiki/Society_and_culture_of_the_Han_dynasty#Competing_ideologies" title="Society and culture of the Han dynasty">Huang-Lao Daoism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDemi&#233;ville1986821&#8211;822_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDemi&#233;ville1986821&#8211;822-162">[162]</a></sup> China\'s first known Buddhist temple, the <a href="/wiki/White_Horse_Temple" title="White Horse Temple">White Horse Temple</a>, was constructed outside the wall of the capital, <a href="/wiki/Luoyang" title="Luoyang">Luoyang</a>, during Emperor Ming\'s reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDemi&#233;ville1986823_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDemi&#233;ville1986823-163">[163]</a></sup> Important Buddhist canons were translated into Chinese during the 2nd century AD, including the <i><a href="/wiki/Sutra_of_Forty-two_Chapters" title="Sutra of Forty-two Chapters">Sutra of Forty-two Chapters</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perfection_of_Wisdom" title="Perfection of Wisdom">Perfection of Wisdom</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shurangama_Sutra" title="Shurangama Sutra">Shurangama Sutra</a></i>, and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pratyutpanna_Sutra" title="Pratyutpanna Sutra">Pratyutpanna Sutra</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Government_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="Government of the Han dynasty">Government of the Han dynasty</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_emperors_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="List of emperors of the Han dynasty">List of emperors of the Han dynasty</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_government">Central government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Central government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:308px;max-width:308px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pottery_palace.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Pottery_palace.JPG/150px-Pottery_palace.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Pottery_palace.JPG/225px-Pottery_palace.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Pottery_palace.JPG/300px-Pottery_palace.JPG 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pottery_palace_1.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3888" decoding="async" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Pottery_palace_1.JPG/150px-Pottery_palace_1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Pottery_palace_1.JPG/225px-Pottery_palace_1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Pottery_palace_1.JPG/300px-Pottery_palace_1.JPG 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">A pottery model of a palace from a Han-dynasty tomb; the entrances to the emperor\'s palaces were strictly guarded by the Minister of the Guards; if it was found that a commoner, official, or noble entered without explicit permission via a tally system, the intruder was subject to execution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;197268&#8211;69_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;197268&#8211;69-165">[165]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In Han government, the emperor was the supreme judge and lawgiver, the commander-in-chief of the armed forces and sole designator of official nominees appointed to the top posts in central and local administrations; those who earned a <a href="/wiki/Government_of_the_Han_dynasty#Salaries" title="Government of the Han dynasty">600-bushel salary-rank or higher</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-de_Crespigny_2007_1216_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-de_Crespigny_2007_1216-166">[166]</a></sup> Theoretically, there were no limits to his power. However, state organs with competing interests and institutions such as the court conference (<i>tingyi</i> &#24311;&#35696;)&#8212;where ministers were convened to reach <a href="/wiki/Consensus_decision-making" title="Consensus decision-making">majority consensus</a> on an issue&#8212;pressured the emperor to accept the advice of his ministers on policy decisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> If the emperor rejected a court conference decision, he risked alienating his high ministers. Nevertheless, emperors sometimes did reject the majority opinion reached at court conferences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;197270&#8211;71_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;197270&#8211;71-168">[168]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Below the emperor were his <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_(government)" title="Cabinet (government)">cabinet</a> members known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Three_Councillors_of_State" title="Three Councillors of State">Three Councillors of State</a> (<i>San gong</i> &#19977;&#20844;). These were the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_China" title="Chancellor of China">Chancellor</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minister_over_the_Masses" title="Minister over the Masses">Minister over the Masses</a> (<i>Chengxiang</i> &#19998;&#30456; or <i>Da situ</i> &#22823;&#21496;&#24466;), the Imperial Counselor or Excellency of Works (<i>Yushi dafu</i> &#24481;&#21490;&#22823;&#22827; or <i>Da sikong</i> &#22823;&#21496;&#31354;), and Grand Commandant or Grand Marshal (<i>Taiwei</i> &#22826;&#23561; or <i>Da sima</i> &#22823;&#21496;&#39340;).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Chancellor, whose title was changed to \'Minister over the Masses\' in 8 BC, was chiefly responsible for drafting the <a href="/wiki/Government_budget" title="Government budget">government budget</a>. The Chancellor\'s other duties included managing provincial registers for land and population, leading court conferences, acting as judge in lawsuits and recommending nominees for high office. He could appoint officials below the salary-rank of 600 bushels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Imperial Counselor\'s chief duty was to conduct disciplinary procedures for officials. He shared similar duties with the Chancellor, such as receiving annual provincial reports. However, when his title was changed to Minister of Works in 8 BC, his chief duty became oversight of public works projects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Basket_from_Lo-lang.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="496" data-file-width="870" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Basket_from_Lo-lang.jpg/220px-Basket_from_Lo-lang.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Basket_from_Lo-lang.jpg/330px-Basket_from_Lo-lang.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Basket_from_Lo-lang.jpg/440px-Basket_from_Lo-lang.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Basket_from_Lo-lang.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A scene of historic paragons of filial piety conversing with one another, Chinese painted artwork on a <a href="/wiki/Lacquerware" title="Lacquerware">lacquered</a> basketwork box, excavated from an Eastern-Han tomb of what was the Chinese <a href="/wiki/Lelang_Commandery" title="Lelang Commandery">Lelang Commandery</a> in modern <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Grand Commandant, whose title was changed to Grand Marshal in 119 BC before reverting to Grand Commandant in 51 AD, was the irregularly posted commander of the military and then <a href="/wiki/Regent" title="Regent">regent</a> during the Western Han period. In the Eastern Han era he was chiefly a civil official who shared many of the same censorial powers as the other two Councillors of State.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ranked below the Three Councillors of State were the <a href="/wiki/Nine_Ministers" title="Nine Ministers">Nine Ministers</a> (<i>Jiu qing</i> &#20061;&#21375;), who each headed a specialized ministry. The Minister of Ceremonies (<i>Taichang</i> &#22826;&#24120;) was the chief official in charge of religious rites, rituals, prayers and the maintenance of ancestral temples and altars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> The Minister of the Household (<i>Guang lu xun</i> &#20809;&#31103;&#21235;) was in charge of the emperor\'s security within the palace grounds, external imperial parks and wherever the emperor made an outing by chariot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> The Minister of the Guards (<i>Weiwei</i> &#34907;&#23561;) was responsible for securing and patrolling the walls, towers, and gates of the imperial palaces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> The Minister Coachman (<i>Taipu</i> &#22826;&#20693;) was responsible for the maintenance of imperial stables, horses, carriages and coach-houses for the emperor and his palace attendants, as well as the supply of horses for the armed forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> The Minister of Justice (<i>Tingwei</i> &#24311;&#23561;) was the chief official in charge of upholding, administering, and interpreting the law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> The Minister Herald (<i>Da honglu</i> &#22823;&#40251;&#33242;) was the chief official in charge of receiving honored guests at the imperial court, such as nobles and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Imperial_China" title="Foreign relations of Imperial China">foreign ambassadors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> The Minister of the Imperial Clan (<i>Zongzheng</i> &#23447;&#27491;) oversaw the imperial court\'s interactions with the empire\'s nobility and extended imperial family, such as granting fiefs and titles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> The Minister of Finance (<i>Da sinong</i> &#22823;&#21496;&#36786;) was the <a href="/wiki/Treasurer" title="Treasurer">treasurer</a> for the official bureaucracy and the armed forces who handled tax revenues and set standards for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Units_of_measurement" title="Units of measurement">units of measurement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> The Minister Steward (<i>Shaofu</i> &#23569;&#24220;) served the emperor exclusively, providing him with entertainment and amusements, proper food and clothing, medicine and physical care, valuables and equipment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Local_government">Local government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Local government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:316px;max-width:316px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Woman_with_a_mirror,_China,_unearthed_at_Songjialin,_Pixian,_Sichuan,_Eastern_Han_dynasty,_25-220_AD,_ceramic_-_Sichuan_Provincial_Museum_-_Chengdu,_China_-_DSC04768.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="5472" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Woman_with_a_mirror%2C_China%2C_unearthed_at_Songjialin%2C_Pixian%2C_Sichuan%2C_Eastern_Han_dynasty%2C_25-220_AD%2C_ceramic_-_Sichuan_Provincial_Museum_-_Chengdu%2C_China_-_DSC04768.jpg/150px-thumbnail.jpg" 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Woman_with_a_mirror%2C_China%2C_unearthed_at_Songjialin%2C_Pixian%2C_Sichuan%2C_Eastern_Han_dynasty%2C_25-220_AD%2C_ceramic_-_Sichuan_Provincial_Museum_-_Chengdu%2C_China_-_DSC04768.jpg/300px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:160px;max-width:160px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pottery_dog_1.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="539" data-file-width="376" decoding="async" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Pottery_dog_1.JPG/158px-Pottery_dog_1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Pottery_dog_1.JPG/237px-Pottery_dog_1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Pottery_dog_1.JPG/316px-Pottery_dog_1.JPG 2x" width="158"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><b>Left:</b> a <a href="/wiki/Chinese_ceramics" title="Chinese ceramics">Chinese ceramic</a> statue of a seated woman holding a <a href="/wiki/Bronze_mirror" title="Bronze mirror">bronze mirror</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="History of the Han dynasty">Eastern Han period</a> (25&#8211;220 AD), <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan Provincial Museum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chengdu" title="Chengdu">Chengdu</a><br/><b>Right:</b> a pottery dog found in a Han tomb wearing a decorative <a href="/wiki/Dog_collar" title="Dog collar">dog collar</a>, indicating their <a href="/wiki/Dogs_in_ancient_China" title="Dogs in ancient China">domestication as pets</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWang198257,_203_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWang198257,_203-182">[182]</a></sup> while it is known from written sources that the emperor\'s imperial parks had kennels for keeping <a href="/wiki/Hunting_dog" title="Hunting dog">hunting dogs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBielenstein198083_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBielenstein198083-183">[183]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In the Han dynasty, excluding kingdoms and marquessates, was divided, in descending order of size, into political units of <a href="/wiki/Zhou_(country_subdivision)" title="Zhou (country subdivision)">provinces</a> (<i>zhou</i>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commandery_(China)" title="Commandery (China)">commanderies</a> (<i>jun</i>), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Counties_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China#History" title="Counties of the People\'s Republic of China">counties</a> (<i>xian</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Crespigny20071228_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Crespigny20071228-184">[184]</a></sup> A county was divided into several <a href="/wiki/District_(China)" title="District (China)">districts</a>, the latter composed of a group of <a href="/wiki/Hamlet_(place)" title="Hamlet (place)">hamlets</a>, each containing about a hundred families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBielenstein1980103_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBielenstein1980103-185">[185]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENishijima1986551&#8211;552_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENishijima1986551&#8211;552-186">[186]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The heads of provinces, whose official title was changed from Inspector to Governor and vice versa several times during Han, were responsible for inspecting several commandery-level and kingdom-level administrations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> On the basis of their reports, the officials in these local administrations would be promoted, demoted, dismissed or prosecuted by the imperial court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBielenstein198091_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBielenstein198091-188">[188]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A governor could take various actions without permission from the imperial court. The lower-ranked inspector had executive powers only during times of crisis, such as raising militias across the commanderies under his jurisdiction to suppress a rebellion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Crespigny20071228_184-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Crespigny20071228-184">[184]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A commandery consisted of a group of counties, and was headed by an Administrator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Crespigny20071228_184-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Crespigny20071228-184">[184]</a></sup> He was the top civil and military leader of the commandery and handled defense, lawsuits, seasonal instructions to farmers and recommendations of nominees for office sent annually to the capital in a quota system first established by Emperor Wu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> The head of a large county of about 10,000 households was called a Prefect, while the heads of smaller counties were called Chiefs, and both could be referred to as <a href="/wiki/Magistrate" title="Magistrate">Magistrates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> A Magistrate maintained law and order in his county, registered the populace for taxation, mobilized commoners for annual <a href="/wiki/Corv%C3%A9e" title="Corv&#233;e">corv&#233;e</a> duties, repaired schools and supervised public works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBielenstein1980100_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBielenstein1980100-191">[191]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kingdoms_and_marquessates">Kingdoms and marquessates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Kingdoms and marquessates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kings_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="Kings of the Han dynasty">Kings of the Han dynasty</a></div>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:308px;max-width:308px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wall-Painting_from_a_Han_dynasty_Tomb_in_Tung-p%27ing_County.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="470" data-file-width="676" decoding="async" height="104" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Wall-Painting_from_a_Han_dynasty_Tomb_in_Tung-p%27ing_County.jpg/150px-Wall-Painting_from_a_Han_dynasty_Tomb_in_Tung-p%27ing_County.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Wall-Painting_from_a_Han_dynasty_Tomb_in_Tung-p%27ing_County.jpg/225px-Wall-Painting_from_a_Han_dynasty_Tomb_in_Tung-p%27ing_County.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Wall-Painting_from_a_Han_dynasty_Tomb_in_Tung-p%27ing_County.jpg/300px-Wall-Painting_from_a_Han_dynasty_Tomb_in_Tung-p%27ing_County.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image"
