href="/wiki/File:Queen_Mother_of_the_West_from_a_Wall-Painting_in_Han_Dynasty_Tomb.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Queen_Mother_of_the_West_from_a_Wall-Painting_in_Han_Dynasty_Tomb.jpg/150px-Queen_Mother_of_the_West_from_a_Wall-Painting_in_Han_Dynasty_Tomb.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Queen_Mother_of_the_West_from_a_Wall-Painting_in_Han_Dynasty_Tomb.jpg/225px-Queen_Mother_of_the_West_from_a_Wall-Painting_in_Han_Dynasty_Tomb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Queen_Mother_of_the_West_from_a_Wall-Painting_in_Han_Dynasty_Tomb.jpg/300px-Queen_Mother_of_the_West_from_a_Wall-Painting_in_Han_Dynasty_Tomb.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="History of the Han dynasty">Late Western Han</a> (202 BC&#8211;9 AD) or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xin_Dynasty" title="Xin Dynasty">Xin Dynasty</a> (9&#8211;25 AD) wall <a href="/wiki/Mural" title="Mural">murals</a> showing men and women dressed in <i><a href="/wiki/Hanfu" title="Hanfu">hanfu</a></i>, with the <a href="/wiki/Queen_Mother_of_the_West" title="Queen Mother of the West">Queen Mother of the West</a> dressed in <i><a href="/wiki/Shenyi" title="Shenyi">shenyi</a></i>, from a tomb in <a href="/wiki/Dongping_County" title="Dongping County">Dongping County</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shandong_province" title="Shandong province">Shandong province</a>, China</div></div></div>\n<p>Kingdoms&#8212;roughly the size of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commandery_(China)" title="Commandery (China)">commanderies</a>&#8212;were ruled exclusively by the emperor\'s male relatives as semi-autonomous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fiefdom" title="Fiefdom">fiefdoms</a>. Before 157 BC some kingdoms were ruled by non-relatives, granted to them in return for their services to Emperor Gaozu. The administration of each kingdom was very similar to that of the central government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> Although the emperor appointed the Chancellor of each kingdom, kings appointed all the remaining civil officials in their fiefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, in 145 BC, after several insurrections by the kings, Emperor Jing removed the kings\' rights to appoint officials whose <a href="/wiki/Government_of_the_Han_dynasty#Salaries" title="Government of the Han dynasty">salaries were higher than 400 bushels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBielenstein1980105&#8211;106_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBielenstein1980105&#8211;106-194">[194]</a></sup> The Imperial Counselors and Nine Ministers (excluding the Minister Coachman) of every kingdom were abolished, although the Chancellor was still appointed by the central government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBielenstein1980105&#8211;106_194-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBielenstein1980105&#8211;106-194">[194]</a></sup>\n</p><p>With these reforms, kings were reduced to being nominal heads of their fiefs, gaining a personal income from only a portion of the taxes collected in their kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;197276_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;197276-195">[195]</a></sup> Similarly, the officials in the administrative staff of a full marquess\'s fief were appointed by the central government. A marquess\'s Chancellor was ranked as the equivalent of a county Prefect. Like a king, the marquess collected a portion of the tax revenues in his fief as personal income.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EasternHan-ColouredPotteryFigurine-ShanghaiMuseum-May27-08.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2994" data-file-width="1434" decoding="async" height="355" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/EasternHan-ColouredPotteryFigurine-ShanghaiMuseum-May27-08.jpg/170px-EasternHan-ColouredPotteryFigurine-ShanghaiMuseum-May27-08.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/EasternHan-ColouredPotteryFigurine-ShanghaiMuseum-May27-08.jpg/255px-EasternHan-ColouredPotteryFigurine-ShanghaiMuseum-May27-08.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/EasternHan-ColouredPotteryFigurine-ShanghaiMuseum-May27-08.jpg/340px-EasternHan-ColouredPotteryFigurine-ShanghaiMuseum-May27-08.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EasternHan-ColouredPotteryFigurine-ShanghaiMuseum-May27-08.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Han dynasty era pottery soldier, with a now-faded coating of paint, is missing a weapon.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Military">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Army_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="Army of the Han dynasty">Army of the Han dynasty</a></div>\n<p>At the beginning of the Han dynasty, every male commoner aged twenty-three was liable for <a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">conscription</a> into the military. The minimum age for the military draft was reduced to twenty after <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Zhao_of_Han" title="Emperor Zhao of Han">Emperor Zhao\'s</a> (r.&#160;87&#8211;74 BC) reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChang200770&#8211;71_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChang200770&#8211;71-197">[197]</a></sup> Conscripted soldiers underwent one year of training and one year of service as non-professional soldiers. The year of training was served in one of three branches of the armed forces: <a href="/wiki/Infantry" title="Infantry">infantry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cavalry" title="Cavalry">cavalry</a> or <a href="/wiki/Navy" title="Navy">navy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nishijima_1986_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nishijima_1986-198">[198]</a></sup> The year of active service was served either on the frontier, in a king\'s court or under the Minister of the Guards in the capital. A small professional (paid) standing army was stationed near the capital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nishijima_1986_198-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nishijima_1986-198">[198]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the Eastern Han, conscription could be avoided if one paid a commutable tax. The Eastern Han court favored the recruitment of a <a href="/wiki/Volunteer_military" title="Volunteer military">volunteer army</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Crespigny2007564&#8211;565,_1234_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Crespigny2007564&#8211;565,_1234-199">[199]</a></sup> The volunteer army comprised the Southern Army (<i>Nanjun</i> &#21335;&#36557;), while the <a href="/wiki/Standing_army" title="Standing army">standing army</a> stationed in and near the capital was the Northern Army (<i>Beijun</i> &#21271;&#36557;).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBielenstein1980114&#8211;115_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBielenstein1980114&#8211;115-200">[200]</a></sup> Led by Colonels (<i>Xiaowei</i> &#26657;&#23561;), the Northern Army consisted of five regiments, each composed of several thousand soldiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> When central authority collapsed after 189 AD, wealthy landowners, members of the aristocracy/nobility, and regional military-governors relied upon their retainers to act as their own personal troops (<i>buqu</i> &#37096;&#26354;).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;1972132&#8211;133_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECh\'&#252;1972132&#8211;133-202">[202]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During times of war, the volunteer army was increased, and a much larger <a href="/wiki/Militia" title="Militia">militia</a> was raised across the country to supplement the Northern Army. In these circumstances, a General (<i>Jiangjun</i> &#23559;&#36557;) led a <a href="/wiki/Division_(military)" title="Division (military)">division</a>, which was divided into <a href="/wiki/Regiment" title="Regiment">regiments</a> led by Colonels and sometimes Majors (<i>Sima</i> &#21496;&#39340;). Regiments were divided into <a href="/wiki/Company_(military_unit)" title="Company (military unit)">companies</a> and led by Captains. <a href="/wiki/Platoon" title="Platoon">Platoons</a> were the smallest units of soldiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="Economy of the Han dynasty">Economy of the Han dynasty</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Variations_in_currency">Variations in currency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Variations in currency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:S-114_W_Han_wuzhu,_Han_Wudi,_140-87,_25_5mm.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="436" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/S-114_W_Han_wuzhu%2C_Han_Wudi%2C_140-87%2C_25_5mm.jpg/220px-S-114_W_Han_wuzhu%2C_Han_Wudi%2C_140-87%2C_25_5mm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/S-114_W_Han_wuzhu%2C_Han_Wudi%2C_140-87%2C_25_5mm.jpg/330px-S-114_W_Han_wuzhu%2C_Han_Wudi%2C_140-87%2C_25_5mm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/S-114_W_Han_wuzhu%2C_Han_Wudi%2C_140-87%2C_25_5mm.jpg/440px-S-114_W_Han_wuzhu%2C_Han_Wudi%2C_140-87%2C_25_5mm.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:S-114_W_Han_wuzhu,_Han_Wudi,_140-87,_25_5mm.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <i>wuzhu</i> (&#20116;&#37526;) coin issued during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wu_of_Han" title="Emperor Wu of Han">Emperor Wu</a> (r. 141&#8211;87 BC), 25.5&#160;mm in diameter</div></div></div>\n<p>The Han dynasty inherited the <i><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Chinese_coinage#Ban_Liang_coins" title="Ancient Chinese coinage">ban liang</a></i> coin type from the Qin. In the beginning of the Han, Emperor Gaozu closed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mint_(coin)" title="Mint (coin)">government mint</a> in favor of private minting of coins. This decision was reversed in 186 BC by his widow <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empress_L%C3%BC_Zhi" title="Empress L&#252; Zhi">Grand Empress Dowager L&#252; Zhi</a> (d. 180 BC), who abolished private minting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENishijima1986586_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENishijima1986586-204">[204]</a></sup> In 182 BC, L&#252; Zhi issued a bronze coin that was much lighter in weight than previous coins. This caused widespread <a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">inflation</a> that was not reduced until 175 BC when Emperor Wen allowed private minters to manufacture coins that were precisely 2.6&#160;g (0.09&#160;oz) in weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENishijima1986586_204-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENishijima1986586-204">[204]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 144 BC Emperor Jing abolished private minting in favor of central-government and commandery-level minting; he also introduced a new coin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENishijima1986586&#8211;587_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENishijima1986586&#8211;587-205">[205]</a></sup> Emperor Wu introduced another in 120 BC, but a year later he abandoned the <i>ban liangs</i> entirely in favor of the <i><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Chinese_coinage#Western_Han_and_the_Wu_Zhu_coins" title="Ancient Chinese coinage">wuzhu</a></i> (&#20116;&#37526;) coin, weighing 3.2&#160;g (0.11&#160;oz).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENishijima1986587_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENishijima1986587-206">[206]</a></sup> The <i>wuzhu</i> became China\'s standard coin until the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a> (618&#8211;907 AD). Its use was interrupted briefly by several new currencies introduced during Wang Mang\'s regime until it was reinstated in 40 AD by Emperor Guangwu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since commandery-issued coins were often of inferior quality and lighter weight, the central government closed commandery mints and monopolized the issue of coinage in 113 BC. This Central government issuance of coinage was overseen by the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_the_Han_dynasty#Superintendent_of_Waterways_and_Parks" title="Government of the Han dynasty">Superintendent of Waterways and Parks</a>, this duty being transferred to the Minister of Finance during Eastern Han.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxation_and_property">Taxation and property</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Taxation and property">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Aside from the landowner\'s <a href="/wiki/Land_value_tax#History" title="Land value tax">land tax</a> paid in a portion of their <a href="/wiki/Crop_yield" title="Crop yield">crop yield</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tax_per_head" title="Tax per head">poll tax</a> and <a href="/wiki/Property_tax" title="Property tax">property taxes</a> were paid in coin cash.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENishijima1986600&#8211;601_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENishijima1986600&#8211;601-209">[209]</a></sup> The annual poll tax rate for adult men and women was 120 coins and 20 coins for minors. Merchants were required to pay a higher rate of 240 coins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENishijima1986598_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENishijima1986598-210">[210]</a></sup> The poll tax stimulated a money economy that necessitated the minting of over 28,000,000,000 coins from 118 BC to 5 AD, an average of 220,000,000 coins a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENishijima1986588_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENishijima1986588-211">[211]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:308px;max-width:308px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:143px;max-width:143px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Earthenware_architecture_models,_Eastern_Han_Dynasty,_12.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="106" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Earthenware_architecture_models%2C_Eastern_Han_Dynasty%2C_12.JPG/141px-Earthenware_architecture_models%2C_Eastern_Han_Dynasty%2C_12.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Earthenware_architecture_models%2C_Eastern_Han_Dynasty%2C_12.JPG/212px-Earthenware_architecture_models%2C_Eastern_Han_Dynasty%2C_12.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Earthenware_architecture_models%2C_Eastern_Han_Dynasty%2C_12.JPG/282px-Earthenware_architecture_models%2C_Eastern_Han_Dynasty%2C_12.JPG 2x" width="141"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:161px;max-width:161px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pottery_tower_6.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3888" decoding="async" height="106" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Pottery_tower_6.JPG/159px-Pottery_tower_6.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Pottery_tower_6.JPG/239px-Pottery_tower_6.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Pottery_tower_6.JPG/318px-Pottery_tower_6.JPG 2x" width="159"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><b>Left image</b>: Eastern-Han tomb models of towers with <i><a href="/wiki/Dougong" title="Dougong">dougong</a></i> brackets supporting balconies, 1st&#8211;2nd century AD. <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Heng" title="Zhang Heng">Zhang Heng</a> (78&#8211;139 AD) described the large imperial park in the suburbs of Chang\'an as having tall towers where archers would shoot stringed arrows from the top in order to entertain the Western Han emperors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBulling1962312_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBulling1962312-212">[212]</a></sup><br/> <b>Right image</b>: A painted <a href="/wiki/Chinese_ceramics" title="Chinese ceramics">ceramic</a> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_architecture" title="Chinese architecture">architectural</a> model&#8212;found in an Eastern-Han tomb at Jiazuo, Henan province&#8212;depicting a fortified manor with towers, a <a href="/wiki/Courtyard" title="Courtyard">courtyard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Veranda" title="Veranda">verandas</a>, tiled rooftops, <i><a href="/wiki/Dougong" title="Dougong">dougong</a></i> support brackets, and a <a href="/wiki/Covered_bridge" title="Covered bridge">covered bridge</a> extending from the third floor of the main tower to the smaller watchtower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuo200546&#8211;48_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuo200546&#8211;48-213">[213]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The widespread circulation of coin cash allowed successful merchants to invest money in land, empowering the very social class the government attempted to suppress through heavy commercial and property taxes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENishijima1986601_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENishijima1986601-214">[214]</a></sup> Emperor Wu even enacted laws which banned registered merchants from owning land, yet powerful merchants were able to avoid registration and own large tracts of land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The small landowner-cultivators formed the majority of the Han tax base; this revenue was threatened during the latter half of Eastern Han when many peasants fell into debt and were forced to work as farming tenants for wealthy <a href="/wiki/Landlord" title="Landlord">landlords</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup> The Han government enacted reforms in order to keep small landowner-cultivators out of debt and on their own farms. These reforms included reducing taxes, temporary remissions of taxes, granting loans and providing landless peasants temporary lodging and work in <a href="/wiki/Tuntian" title="Tuntian">agricultural colonies</a> until they could recover from their debts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 168 BC, the land tax rate was reduced from one-fifteenth of a farming household\'s crop yield to one-thirtieth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup> and later to a one-hundredth of a crop yield for the last decades of the dynasty. The consequent loss of government revenue was compensated for by increasing property taxes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENishijima1986596&#8211;598_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENishijima1986596&#8211;598-219">[219]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The labor tax took the form of <a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">conscripted labor</a> for one month per year, which was imposed upon male commoners aged fifteen to fifty-six. This could be avoided in Eastern Han with a commutable tax, since hired labor became more popular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Private_manufacture_and_government_monopolies">Private manufacture and government monopolies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Private manufacture and government monopolies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iron_ji_and_knife,_Han_Dynasty.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="427" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Iron_ji_and_knife%2C_Han_Dynasty.JPG/220px-Iron_ji_and_knife%2C_Han_Dynasty.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Iron_ji_and_knife%2C_Han_Dynasty.JPG/330px-Iron_ji_and_knife%2C_Han_Dynasty.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Iron_ji_and_knife%2C_Han_Dynasty.JPG/440px-Iron_ji_and_knife%2C_Han_Dynasty.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iron_ji_and_knife,_Han_Dynasty.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Han-dynasty iron <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ji_(halberd)" title="Ji (halberd)">Ji (halberd)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_swords" title="Chinese swords">iron dagger</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In the early Western Han, a wealthy salt or iron industrialist, whether a semi-autonomous king or wealthy merchant, could boast funds that rivaled the imperial <a href="/wiki/Treasury" title="Treasury">treasury</a> and amass a peasant workforce of over a thousand. This kept many peasants away from their farms and denied the government a significant portion of its land tax revenue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> To eliminate the influence of such private entrepreneurs, Emperor Wu nationalized the salt and iron industries in 117 BC and allowed many of the former industrialists to become officials administering the state monopolies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup> By Eastern Han times, the central government monopolies were repealed in favor of production by commandery and county administrations, as well as private businessmen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Liquor" title="Liquor">Liquor</a> was another profitable private industry nationalized by the central government in 98 BC. However, this was repealed in 81 BC and a property tax rate of two coins for every 0.2&#160;L (0.05 gallons) was levied for those who traded it privately.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> By 110 BC Emperor Wu also interfered with the profitable trade in grain when he eliminated <a href="/wiki/Speculation" title="Speculation">speculation</a> by selling government-stored grain at a lower price than demanded by merchants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey199975_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey199975-225">[225]</a></sup> Apart from Emperor Ming\'s creation of a short-lived Office for Price Adjustment and Stabilization, which was abolished in 68 AD, central-government price control regulations were largely absent during the Eastern Han.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Crespigny2007605_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Crespigny2007605-226">[226]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_and_technology">Science and technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Science and technology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Science_and_technology_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="Science and technology of the Han dynasty">Science and technology of the Han dynasty</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Summer_Vacation_2007,_263,_Watchtower_In_The_Morning_Light,_Dunhuang,_Gansu_Province.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2561" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Summer_Vacation_2007%2C_263%2C_Watchtower_In_The_Morning_Light%2C_Dunhuang%2C_Gansu_Province.jpg/220px-Summer_Vacation_2007%2C_263%2C_Watchtower_In_The_Morning_Light%2C_Dunhuang%2C_Gansu_Province.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Summer_Vacation_2007%2C_263%2C_Watchtower_In_The_Morning_Light%2C_Dunhuang%2C_Gansu_Province.jpg/330px-Summer_Vacation_2007%2C_263%2C_Watchtower_In_The_Morning_Light%2C_Dunhuang%2C_Gansu_Province.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Summer_Vacation_2007%2C_263%2C_Watchtower_In_The_Morning_Light%2C_Dunhuang%2C_Gansu_Province.jpg/440px-Summer_Vacation_2007%2C_263%2C_Watchtower_In_The_Morning_Light%2C_Dunhuang%2C_Gansu_Province.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Summer_Vacation_2007,_263,_Watchtower_In_The_Morning_Light,_Dunhuang,_Gansu_Province.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The ruins of a Han-dynasty <a href="/wiki/Watchtower" title="Watchtower">watchtower</a> made of <a href="/wiki/Rammed_earth" title="Rammed earth">rammed earth</a> at <a href="/wiki/Dunhuang" title="Dunhuang">Dunhuang</a>, Gansu province, the eastern edge of the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Han dynasty was a unique period in the development of premodern Chinese science and technology, comparable to the level of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Technology_of_the_Song_dynasty" title="Technology of the Song dynasty">scientific and technological growth</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> (960&#8211;1279).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_materials">Writing materials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Writing materials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In the 1st millennium BC, typical ancient Chinese writing materials were <a href="/wiki/Chinese_bronze_inscriptions" title="Chinese bronze inscriptions">bronzewares</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oracle_bone_script" title="Oracle bone script">animal bones</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bamboo_slip" title="Bamboo slip">bamboo slips</a> or wooden boards. By the beginning of the Han dynasty, the chief writing materials were <a href="/wiki/Clay_tablet" title="Clay tablet">clay tablets</a>, silk cloth, hemp\'s paper,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnpaper_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnpaper-228">[228]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup> and rolled scrolls made from <a href="/wiki/Bamboo" title="Bamboo">bamboo</a> strips sewn together with hempen string; these were passed through drilled holes and secured with clay stamps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The oldest known Chinese piece of hard, hempen <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wrapping_paper" title="Wrapping paper">wrapping paper</a> dates to the 2nd century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnpaper_228-1"><a href="#cite_note-cnpaper-228">[228]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup> The standard <a href="/wiki/Papermaking" title="Papermaking">papermaking</a> process was invented by <a href="/wiki/Cai_Lun" title="Cai Lun">Cai Lun</a> (AD 50&#8211;121) in 105.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup> The oldest known surviving piece of <a href="/wiki/Paper" title="Paper">paper</a> with writing on it was found in the ruins of a Han <a href="/wiki/Watchtower" title="Watchtower">watchtower</a> that had been abandoned in AD 110, in <a href="/wiki/Inner_Mongolia" title="Inner Mongolia">Inner Mongolia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECotterell200411_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECotterell200411-233">[233]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metallurgy_and_agriculture">Metallurgy and agriculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Metallurgy and agriculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Evidence suggests that <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">blast furnaces</a>, that convert raw <a href="/wiki/Iron_ore" title="Iron ore">iron ore</a> into <a href="/wiki/Pig_iron" title="Pig iron">pig iron</a>, which can be remelted in a <a href="/wiki/Cupola_furnace" title="Cupola furnace">cupola furnace</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Cast_iron" title="Cast iron">cast iron</a> by means of a <a href="/wiki/Cold_blast" title="Cold blast">cold blast</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hot_blast" title="Hot blast">hot blast</a>, were operational in China by the late <a href="/wiki/Spring_and_Autumn_period" title="Spring and Autumn period">Spring and Autumn period</a> (722&#8211;481 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Bloomery" title="Bloomery">bloomery</a> was nonexistent in ancient China; however, the Han-era Chinese produced <a href="/wiki/Wrought_iron" title="Wrought iron">wrought iron</a> by injecting excess <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> into a furnace and causing <a href="/wiki/Decarburization" title="Decarburization">decarburization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPigott1999177,_191_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPigott1999177,_191-235">[235]</a></sup> Cast iron and pig iron could be converted into wrought iron and <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> using a <a href="/wiki/Finery_forge" title="Finery forge">fining</a> process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iron_scissors,_E_Han.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="466" data-file-width="330" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Iron_scissors%2C_E_Han.JPG/170px-Iron_scissors%2C_E_Han.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Iron_scissors%2C_E_Han.JPG/255px-Iron_scissors%2C_E_Han.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/16/Iron_scissors%2C_E_Han.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iron_scissors,_E_Han.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A pair of Eastern-Han iron <a href="/wiki/Scissors" title="Scissors">scissors</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Han dynasty Chinese used bronze and iron to make a range of weapons, culinary tools, carpenters\' tools and domestic wares.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup> A significant product of these improved iron-smelting techniques was the manufacture of new agricultural tools. The three-legged iron <a href="/wiki/Seed_drill" title="Seed drill">seed drill</a>, invented by the 2nd century BC, enabled farmers to carefully plant crops in rows instead of <a href="/wiki/Sowing" title="Sowing">casting seeds out by hand</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup> The heavy moldboard iron <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plow" title="Plow">plow</a>, also invented during the Han dynasty, required only one man to control it, two oxen to pull it. It had three <a href="/wiki/Plowshare" title="Plowshare">plowshares</a>, a seed box for the drills, a tool which turned down the soil and could sow roughly 45,730 m<sup>2</sup> (11.3 acres) of land in a single day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup>\n</p><p>To protect crops from wind and drought, the grain intendant Zhao Guo (&#36249;&#36942;) created the alternating fields system (<i>daitianfa</i> &#20195;&#30000;&#27861;) during Emperor Wu\'s reign. This system switched the positions of <a href="/wiki/Ridge_and_furrow" title="Ridge and furrow">furrows and ridges</a> between growing seasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENishijima1986561&#8211;563_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENishijima1986561&#8211;563-240">[240]</a></sup> Once experiments with this system yielded successful results, the government officially sponsored it and encouraged peasants to use it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENishijima1986561&#8211;563_240-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENishijima1986561&#8211;563-240">[240]</a></sup> Han farmers also used the pit field system (<i>aotian</i> &#20985;&#30000;) for growing crops, which involved heavily fertilized pits that did not require plows or oxen and could be placed on sloping terrain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup> In southern and small parts of central Han-era China, <a href="/wiki/Paddy_field" title="Paddy field">paddy fields</a> were chiefly used to grow rice, while farmers along the <a href="/wiki/Huai_River" title="Huai River">Huai River</a> used <a href="/wiki/Transplanting" title="Transplanting">transplantation</a> methods of rice production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENishijima1986568&#8211;572_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENishijima1986568&#8211;572-242">[242]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Structural_and_geotechnical_engineering">Structural and geotechnical engineering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Structural and geotechnical engineering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gao_Yi_Que2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3057" data-file-width="2392" decoding="async" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Gao_Yi_Que2.jpg/220px-Gao_Yi_Que2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Gao_Yi_Que2.jpg/330px-Gao_Yi_Que2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Gao_Yi_Que2.jpg/440px-Gao_Yi_Que2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gao_Yi_Que2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A stone-carved pillar-gate, or <a href="/wiki/Que_(tower)" title="Que (tower)"><i>que</i> (&#38357;)</a>, 6&#160;m (20&#160;ft) in total height, located at the tomb of Gao Yi in <a href="/wiki/Ya%27an" title="Ya\'an">Ya\'an</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan</a> province, Eastern Han dynasty<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiu200255_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiu200255-243">[243]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty_tomb_architecture" title="Han dynasty tomb architecture">Han dynasty tomb architecture</a></div>\n<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timber" title="Timber">Timber</a> was the chief building material during the Han dynasty; it was used to build palace halls, multi-story residential towers and halls and single-story houses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey199976_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey199976-244">[244]</a></sup> Because wood decays rapidly, the only remaining evidence of Han wooden architecture is a collection of scattered ceramic roof tiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[245]</a></sup> The oldest surviving wooden halls in China date to the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a> (AD 618&#8211;907).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESteinhardt2004228&#8211;238_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESteinhardt2004228&#8211;238-246">[246]</a></sup> Architectural historian Robert L. Thorp points out the scarcity of Han-era archaeological remains, and claims that often unreliable Han-era literary and artistic sources are used by historians for clues about lost Han architecture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThorp1986360&#8211;378_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThorp1986360&#8211;378-247">[247]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Though Han wooden structures decayed, some Han-dynasty ruins made of brick, stone, and <a href="/wiki/Rammed_earth" title="Rammed earth">rammed earth</a> remain intact. This includes stone pillar-gates, brick tomb chambers, rammed-earth <a href="/wiki/Chinese_city_wall" title="Chinese city wall">city walls</a>, rammed-earth and brick <a href="/wiki/Beacon" title="Beacon">beacon</a> towers, rammed-earth sections of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Wall_of_China" title="Great Wall of China">Great Wall</a>, rammed-earth platforms where elevated halls once stood, and two rammed-earth <a href="/wiki/Castle" title="Castle">castles</a> in <a href="/wiki/Gansu" title="Gansu">Gansu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup> The ruins of rammed-earth walls that once surrounded the capitals Chang\'an and Luoyang still stand, along with their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sewage_collection_and_disposal" title="Sewage collection and disposal">drainage systems</a> of brick arches, ditches, and ceramic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_pipe" title="Water pipe">water pipes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWang19821&#8211;39_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWang19821&#8211;39-249">[249]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Que_(tower)" title="Que (tower)">Monumental stone pillar-gates</a>, twenty-nine of which survive from the Han period, formed entrances of walled enclosures at shrine and tomb sites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup> These pillars feature artistic imitations of wooden and ceramic building components such as roof tiles, eaves, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balustrades" title="Balustrades">balustrades</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[251]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Courtyard_house" title="Courtyard house">courtyard house</a> is the most common type of home portrayed in Han artwork.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey199976_244-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey199976-244">[244]</a></sup> Ceramic architectural <a href="/wiki/Architectural_model" title="Architectural model">models of buildings</a>, like houses and towers, were found in Han tombs, perhaps to provide lodging for the dead in the afterlife. These provide valuable clues about lost wooden architecture. The artistic designs found on ceramic roof tiles of tower models are in some cases exact matches to Han roof tiles found at archaeological sites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESteinhardt2005b283&#8211;284_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESteinhardt2005b283&#8211;284-252">[252]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eastern_Han_tomb,_Luoyang_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="427" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Eastern_Han_tomb%2C_Luoyang_2.jpg/220px-Eastern_Han_tomb%2C_Luoyang_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Eastern_Han_tomb%2C_Luoyang_2.jpg/330px-Eastern_Han_tomb%2C_Luoyang_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Eastern_Han_tomb%2C_Luoyang_2.jpg/440px-Eastern_Han_tomb%2C_Luoyang_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eastern_Han_tomb,_Luoyang_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An Eastern-Han <a href="/wiki/Vault_(architecture)" title="Vault (architecture)">vaulted</a> tomb chamber at <a href="/wiki/Luoyang" title="Luoyang">Luoyang</a> made of small bricks</div></div></div>\n<p>Over ten Han-era underground tombs have been found, many of them featuring <a href="/wiki/Arch" title="Arch">archways</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vault_(architecture)" title="Vault (architecture)">vaulted</a> chambers, and <a href="/wiki/Dome" title="Dome">domed</a> roofs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWang1982175&#8211;178_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWang1982175&#8211;178-253">[253]</a></sup> Underground vaults and domes did not require buttress supports since they were held in place by earthen pits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWatson2000108_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWatson2000108-254">[254]</a></sup> The use of brick vaults and domes in aboveground Han structures is unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWatson2000108_254-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWatson2000108-254">[254]</a></sup>\n</p><p>From Han literary sources, it is known that wooden-trestle <a href="/wiki/Beam_bridge" title="Beam bridge">beam bridges</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arch_bridge" title="Arch bridge">arch bridges</a>, <a href="/wiki/Simple_suspension_bridge" title="Simple suspension bridge">simple suspension bridges</a>, and floating <a href="/wiki/Pontoon_bridge" title="Pontoon bridge">pontoon bridges</a> existed in Han China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d161&#8211;188_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986d161&#8211;188-255">[255]</a></sup> However, there are only two known references to arch bridges in Han literature,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c171&#8211;172_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c171&#8211;172-256">[256]</a></sup> and only a single Han relief sculpture in Sichuan depicts an arch bridge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiu200256_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiu200256-257">[257]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Shaft_mining" title="Shaft mining">Underground mine shafts</a>, some reaching depths over 100 metres (330&#160;ft), were created for the extraction of metal ores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[258]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Borehole" title="Borehole">Borehole</a> drilling and <a href="/wiki/Derrick" title="Derrick">derricks</a> were used to lift <a href="/wiki/Brine" title="Brine">brine</a> to iron pans where it was distilled into salt. The distillation furnaces were heated by <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> funneled to the surface through <a href="/wiki/Pipeline_transport" title="Pipeline transport">bamboo pipelines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[259]</a></sup> Dangerous amounts of additional gas were siphoned off via <a href="/wiki/Carburetor" title="Carburetor">carburetor</a> chambers and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exhaust_pipe" title="Exhaust pipe">exhaust pipes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[260]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanical_and_hydraulic_engineering">Mechanical and hydraulic engineering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Mechanical and hydraulic engineering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Winnowing_machine_and_tilt_hammer.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="391" data-file-width="614" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Winnowing_machine_and_tilt_hammer.JPG/220px-Winnowing_machine_and_tilt_hammer.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Winnowing_machine_and_tilt_hammer.JPG/330px-Winnowing_machine_and_tilt_hammer.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Winnowing_machine_and_tilt_hammer.JPG/440px-Winnowing_machine_and_tilt_hammer.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Winnowing_machine_and_tilt_hammer.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Han-dynasty pottery model of two men operating a <a href="/wiki/Winnowing" title="Winnowing">winnowing</a> machine with a <a href="/wiki/Crank_(mechanism)" title="Crank (mechanism)">crank handle</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Trip_hammer" title="Trip hammer">tilt hammer</a> used to pound grain.</div></div></div>\n<p>Han-era mechanical engineering comes largely from the choice observational writings of sometimes-disinterested Confucian scholars who generally considered scientific and engineering endeavors to be far beneath them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFraser2014370_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFraser2014370-261">[261]</a></sup> Professional artisan-engineers (<i>jiang</i> &#21280;) did not leave behind detailed records of their work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[262]</a></sup> Han scholars, who often had little or no expertise in mechanical engineering, sometimes provided insufficient information on the various technologies they described.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c2_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c2-263">[263]</a></sup> Nevertheless, some Han literary sources provide crucial information. For example, in 15 BC the philosopher and writer <a href="/wiki/Yang_Xiong_(author)" title="Yang Xiong (author)">Yang Xiong</a> described the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Belt_(mechanical)" title="Belt (mechanical)">belt drive</a> for a <a href="/wiki/Quilling" title="Quilling">quilling</a> machine, which was of great importance to early textile manufacturing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1988207&#8211;208_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1988207&#8211;208-264">[264]</a></sup> The inventions of mechanical engineer and craftsman <a href="/wiki/Ding_Huan" title="Ding Huan">Ding Huan</a> (&#19969;&#32233;) are mentioned in the <i>Miscellaneous Notes on the Western Capital</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbieri-Low2007197_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbieri-Low2007197-265">[265]</a></sup> Around AD 180, Ding created a manually operated rotary fan used for <a href="/wiki/Air_conditioning" title="Air conditioning">air conditioning</a> within palace buildings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c99,_134,_151,_233_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c99,_134,_151,_233-266">[266]</a></sup> Ding also used <a href="/wiki/Gimbal" title="Gimbal">gimbals</a> as pivotal supports for one of his incense burners and invented the world\'s first known <a href="/wiki/Zoetrope" title="Zoetrope">zoetrope</a> lamp.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b123,_233&#8211;234_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986b123,_233&#8211;234-267">[267]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Modern archaeology has led to the discovery of Han artwork portraying inventions which were otherwise absent in Han literary sources. As observed in Han miniature tomb models, but not in literary sources, the <a href="/wiki/Crank_(mechanism)" title="Crank (mechanism)">crank handle</a> was used to operate the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fan_(mechanical)" title="Fan (mechanical)">fans</a> of <a href="/wiki/Winnowing" title="Winnowing">winnowing</a> machines that separated grain from <a href="/wiki/Chaff" title="Chaff">chaff</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c116&#8211;119,_Plate_CLVI_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c116&#8211;119,_Plate_CLVI-268">[268]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Odometer" title="Odometer">odometer</a> cart, invented during Han, measured journey lengths, using mechanical figures banging drums and gongs to indicate each distance traveled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c281&#8211;285_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c281&#8211;285-269">[269]</a></sup> This invention is depicted in Han artwork by the 2nd century, yet detailed written descriptions were not offered until the 3rd century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c283&#8211;285_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c283&#8211;285-270">[270]</a></sup> Modern archaeologists have also unearthed specimens of devices used during the Han dynasty, for example a pair of sliding metal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caliper" title="Caliper">calipers</a> used by craftsmen for making minute measurements. These calipers contain inscriptions of the exact day and year they were manufactured. These tools are not mentioned in any Han literary sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELoewe1968195&#8211;196_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELoewe1968195&#8211;196-271">[271]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:EastHanSeismograph.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="552" data-file-width="480" decoding="async" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/EastHanSeismograph.JPG/170px-EastHanSeismograph.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/EastHanSeismograph.JPG/255px-EastHanSeismograph.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/EastHanSeismograph.JPG/340px-EastHanSeismograph.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EastHanSeismograph.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A modern replica of <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Heng" title="Zhang Heng">Zhang Heng</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Seismometer" title="Seismometer">seismometer</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waterwheel" title="Waterwheel">waterwheel</a> appeared in Chinese records during the Han. As mentioned by <a href="/wiki/Huan_Tan" title="Huan Tan">Huan Tan</a> about AD 20, they were used to turn gears that lifted iron <a href="/wiki/Trip_hammer" title="Trip hammer">trip hammers</a>, and were used in pounding, <a href="/wiki/Threshing" title="Threshing">threshing</a> and polishing grain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c183&#8211;184,_390&#8211;392_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c183&#8211;184,_390&#8211;392-272">[272]</a></sup> However, there is no sufficient evidence for the <a href="/wiki/Watermill" title="Watermill">watermill</a> in China until about the 5th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c396&#8211;400_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c396&#8211;400-273">[273]</a></sup> The Nanyang Commandery Administrator and mechanical engineer <a href="/wiki/Du_Shi" title="Du Shi">Du Shi</a> (d.&#160;38 AD) created a waterwheel-powered <a href="/wiki/Reciprocating_motion" title="Reciprocating motion">reciprocator</a> that worked the <a href="/wiki/Bellows" title="Bellows">bellows</a> for the smelting of iron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[274]</a></sup> Waterwheels were also used to power <a href="/wiki/Chain_pump" title="Chain pump">chain pumps</a> that lifted water to raised <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a> ditches. The chain pump was first mentioned in China by the philosopher Wang Chong in his 1st-century <i><a href="/wiki/Lunheng" title="Lunheng">Balanced Discourse</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c89,_110,_342&#8211;344_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c89,_110,_342&#8211;344-275">[275]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Armillary_sphere" title="Armillary sphere">armillary sphere</a>, a three-dimensional representation of the movements in the <a href="/wiki/Celestial_sphere" title="Celestial sphere">celestial sphere</a>, was invented in Han China by the 1st century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a343_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a343-276">[276]</a></sup> Using a <a href="/wiki/Water_clock" title="Water clock">water clock</a>, waterwheel and a series of gears, the Court Astronomer <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Heng" title="Zhang Heng">Zhang Heng</a> (AD 78&#8211;139) was able to mechanically rotate his metal-ringed armillary sphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277">[277]</a></sup> To address the problem of slowed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timekeeping" title="Timekeeping">timekeeping</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Pressure_head" title="Pressure head">pressure head</a> of the inflow water clock, Zhang was the first in China to install an additional tank between the reservoir and inflow vessel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[278]</a></sup> Zhang also invented a device he termed an "earthquake weathervane" (<i>houfeng didong yi</i> &#20505;&#39080;&#22320;&#21205;&#20736;), which the British scientist and historian <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Needham" title="Joseph Needham">Joseph Needham</a> described as "the ancestor of all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seismographs" title="Seismographs">seismographs</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[279]</a></sup> This device was able to detect the exact <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_direction" title="Cardinal direction">cardinal</a> or ordinal direction of <a href="/wiki/Earthquake" title="Earthquake">earthquakes</a> from hundreds of kilometers away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[280]</a></sup> It employed an <a href="/wiki/Inverted_pendulum" title="Inverted pendulum">inverted pendulum</a> that, when disturbed by ground tremors, would trigger a set of gears that dropped a metal ball from one of eight dragon mouths (representing all eight directions) into a metal toad\'s mouth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a626&#8211;631_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a626&#8211;631-281">[281]</a></sup>  The account of this device in the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_the_Later_Han" title="Book of the Later Han">Book of the Later Han</a></i> describes how, on one occasion, one of the metal balls was triggered without any of the observers feeling a disturbance.  Several days later, a messenger arrived bearing news that an earthquake had struck in Longxi Commandery (in modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gansu_Province" title="Gansu Province">Gansu Province</a>), the direction the device had indicated, which forced the officials at court to admit the efficacy of Zhang\'s device.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFraser2014376_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFraser2014376-282">[282]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematics">Mathematics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Mathematics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Three Han mathematical treatises still exist. These are the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_on_Numbers_and_Computation" title="Book on Numbers and Computation">Book on Numbers and Computation</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Zhoubi_Suanjing" title="Zhoubi Suanjing">Arithmetical Classic of the Gnomon and the Circular Paths of Heaven</a></i> and the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nine_Chapters_on_the_Mathematical_Art" title="Nine Chapters on the Mathematical Art">Nine Chapters on the Mathematical Art</a></i>. Han-era mathematical achievements include solving problems with <a href="/wiki/Triangle#Types_of_triangle" title="Triangle">right-angle triangles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Square_root" title="Square root">square roots</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cube_root" title="Cube root">cube roots</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Matrix_(mathematics)" title="Matrix (mathematics)">matrix methods</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[283]</a></sup> finding more accurate <a href="/wiki/Chronology_of_computation_of_%CF%80" title="Chronology of computation of &#960;">approximations for pi</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[284]</a></sup> providing <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_proof" title="Mathematical proof">mathematical proof</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Pythagorean_theorem" title="Pythagorean theorem">Pythagorean theorem</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[285]</a></sup> use of the <a href="/wiki/Fraction_(mathematics)" title="Fraction (mathematics)">decimal fraction</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[286]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gaussian_elimination" title="Gaussian elimination">Gaussian elimination</a> to solve <a href="/wiki/System_of_linear_equations" title="System of linear equations">linear equations</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[287]</a></sup> and continued fractions to find the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Root_of_a_function" title="Root of a function">roots of equations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[288]</a></sup>\n</p><p>One of the Han\'s greatest mathematical advancements was the world\'s first use of <a href="/wiki/Negative_number" title="Negative number">negative numbers</a>. Negative numbers first appeared in the <i>Nine Chapters on the Mathematical Art</i> as black <a href="/wiki/Counting_rods" title="Counting rods">counting rods</a>, where positive numbers were represented by red counting rods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiuFengJiangZheng20039&#8211;10_289-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiuFengJiangZheng20039&#8211;10-289">[289]</a></sup> Negative numbers were also used by the <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greek</a> mathematician <a href="/wiki/Diophantus" title="Diophantus">Diophantus</a> around AD 275, and in the 7th-century <a href="/wiki/Bakhshali_manuscript" title="Bakhshali manuscript">Bakhshali manuscript</a> of <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandhara</a>, South Asia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETeresi200265&#8211;66_290-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETeresi200265&#8211;66-290">[290]</a></sup> but were not widely accepted in Europe until the 16th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiuFengJiangZheng20039&#8211;10_289-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiuFengJiangZheng20039&#8211;10-289">[289]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mold_for_bronze_gear_Han_dynasty.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1120" data-file-width="1201" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Mold_for_bronze_gear_Han_dynasty.jpg/220px-Mold_for_bronze_gear_Han_dynasty.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Mold_for_bronze_gear_Han_dynasty.jpg/330px-Mold_for_bronze_gear_Han_dynasty.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Mold_for_bronze_gear_Han_dynasty.jpg/440px-Mold_for_bronze_gear_Han_dynasty.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mold_for_bronze_gear_Han_dynasty.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Han-dynasty era <a href="/wiki/Molding_(process)" title="Molding (process)">mold</a> for making bronze <a href="/wiki/Gear" title="Gear">gear</a> wheels (<a href="/wiki/Shanghai_Museum" title="Shanghai Museum">Shanghai Museum</a>)</div></div></div>\n<p>The Han applied mathematics to various diverse disciplines. In <a href="/wiki/Musical_tuning" title="Musical tuning">musical tuning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jing_Fang" title="Jing Fang">Jing Fang</a> (78&#8211;37 BC) realized that 53 <a href="/wiki/Perfect_fifth" title="Perfect fifth">perfect fifths</a> was approximate to 31 <a href="/wiki/Octave" title="Octave">octaves</a> while creating a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musical_scale" title="Musical scale">musical scale</a> of 60 tones, calculating the difference at <sup>177147</sup>&#8260;<sub>176776</sub> (the same value of <a href="/wiki/53_equal_temperament" title="53 equal temperament">53 equal temperament</a> discovered by the German mathematician <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Mercator" title="Nicholas Mercator">Nicholas Mercator</a> [1620&#8211;1687], i.e. 3<sup>53</sup>/2<sup>84</sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[291]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomy">Astronomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Astronomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Mathematics were essential in drafting the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_calendar" title="Chinese calendar">astronomical calendar</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Lunisolar_calendar" title="Lunisolar calendar">lunisolar calendar</a> that used the Sun and Moon as time-markers throughout the year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[292]</a></sup> Use of the ancient Sifen calendar (&#21476;&#22235;&#20998;&#26310;), which measured the <a href="/wiki/Tropical_year" title="Tropical year">tropical year</a> at 365<sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>4</sub> days, was replaced in 104 BC with the Taichu calendar (&#22826;&#21021;&#26310;) that measured the tropical year at 365<sup>385</sup>&#8260;<sub>1539</sub> days and the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_month" title="Lunar month">lunar month</a> at 29<sup>43</sup>&#8260;<sub>81</sub> days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeng200567_293-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeng200567-293">[293]</a></sup> However, Emperor Zhang later reinstated the Sifen calendar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Crespigny2007498_294-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Crespigny2007498-294">[294]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Han Chinese astronomers made <a href="/wiki/Star_catalogue" title="Star catalogue">star catalogues</a> and detailed records of comets that appeared in the night sky, including recording the 12 BC appearance of the comet now known as <a href="/wiki/Halley%27s_Comet" title="Halley\'s Comet">Halley\'s Comet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[295]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Han dynasty astronomers adopted a <a href="/wiki/Geocentric_model" title="Geocentric model">geocentric model</a> of the universe, theorizing that it was <a href="/wiki/Celestial_sphere" title="Celestial sphere">shaped like a sphere</a> surrounding the earth in the center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[296]</a></sup> They assumed that the Sun, Moon, and planets were spherical and not disc-shaped. They also thought that the illumination of the Moon and planets was caused by <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a>, that <a href="/wiki/Lunar_eclipse" title="Lunar eclipse">lunar eclipses</a> occurred when the Earth obstructed sunlight falling onto the Moon, and that a <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse" title="Solar eclipse">solar eclipse</a> occurred when the Moon obstructed sunlight from reaching the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a227,_414_297-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a227,_414-297">[297]</a></sup> Although others disagreed with his model, Wang Chong accurately described the <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">water cycle</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a> of water into clouds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a468_298-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a468-298">[298]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Cartography.2C_ships.2C_and_vehicles"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Cartography,_ships,_and_vehicles">Cartography, ships, and vehicles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Cartography, ships, and vehicles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Western_Han_Mawangdui_Silk_Map.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3549" data-file-width="3675" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Western_Han_Mawangdui_Silk_Map.JPG/220px-Western_Han_Mawangdui_Silk_Map.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Western_Han_Mawangdui_Silk_Map.JPG/330px-Western_Han_Mawangdui_Silk_Map.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Western_Han_Mawangdui_Silk_Map.JPG/440px-Western_Han_Mawangdui_Silk_Map.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Western_Han_Mawangdui_Silk_Map.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An early Western Han dynasty silk map found in tomb 3 of <a href="/wiki/Mawangdui" title="Mawangdui">Mawangdui</a>, depicting the Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Changsha" title="Changsha">Changsha</a> and Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Nanyue" title="Nanyue">Nanyue</a> in southern China (note: the south direction is oriented at the top).</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eastern_Han_pottery_boat.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Eastern_Han_pottery_boat.JPG/220px-Eastern_Han_pottery_boat.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Eastern_Han_pottery_boat.JPG/330px-Eastern_Han_pottery_boat.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Eastern_Han_pottery_boat.JPG/440px-Eastern_Han_pottery_boat.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eastern_Han_pottery_boat.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An Eastern Han dynasty pottery ship model with a steering <a href="/wiki/Rudder" title="Rudder">rudder</a> at the stern and <a href="/wiki/Anchor" title="Anchor">anchor</a> at the bow</div></div></div>\n<p>Evidence found in Chinese literature, and archaeological evidence, show that <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">cartography</a> existed in China before the Han.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[299]</a></sup> Some of the earliest Han maps discovered were ink-penned silk maps found amongst the <a href="/wiki/Mawangdui_Silk_Texts" title="Mawangdui Silk Texts">Mawangdui Silk Texts</a> in a 2nd-century-BC tomb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[300]</a></sup> The general <a href="/wiki/Ma_Yuan_(Han_dynasty)" title="Ma Yuan (Han dynasty)">Ma Yuan</a> created the world\'s first known <a href="/wiki/Raised-relief_map" title="Raised-relief map">raised-relief map</a> from rice in the 1st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[301]</a></sup> This date could be revised if the tomb of Emperor <a href="/wiki/Qin_Shi_Huang" title="Qin Shi Huang">Qin Shi Huang</a> is excavated and the account in the <i>Records of the Grand Historian</i> concerning a model map of the empire is proven to be true.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a580&#8211;581_302-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986a580&#8211;581-302">[302]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although the use of the <a href="/wiki/Scale_(map)" title="Scale (map)">graduated scale</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grid_reference" title="Grid reference">grid reference</a> for maps was not thoroughly described until the published work of <a href="/wiki/Pei_Xiu" title="Pei Xiu">Pei Xiu</a> (AD 224&#8211;271), there is evidence that in the early 2nd century, cartographer Zhang Heng was the first to use scales and grids for maps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[303]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Han dynasty Chinese sailed in a variety of ships differing from those of previous eras, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lou_chuan" title="Lou chuan">tower ship</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Junk_(ship)" title="Junk (ship)"><i>junk</i> design</a> was developed and realized during the Han era. Junk ships featured a square-ended <a href="/wiki/Bow_(ship)" title="Bow (ship)">bow</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stern" title="Stern">stern</a>, a flat-bottomed <a href="/wiki/Hull_(watercraft)" title="Hull (watercraft)">hull</a> or <a href="/wiki/Carvel_(boat_building)" title="Carvel (boat building)">carvel-shaped</a> hull with no <a href="/wiki/Keel" title="Keel">keel</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sternpost" title="Sternpost">sternpost</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bulkhead_(partition)" title="Bulkhead (partition)">solid transverse bulkheads</a> in the place of <a href="/wiki/Boat_building" title="Boat building">structural ribs</a> found in Western vessels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[304]</a></sup> Moreover, Han ships were the first in the world to be steered using a <a href="/wiki/Rudder" title="Rudder">rudder</a> at the stern, in contrast to the simpler <a href="/wiki/Steering_oar" title="Steering oar">steering oar</a> used for riverine transport,  allowing them to sail on the high seas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305"><a href="#cite_note-305">[305]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although ox-carts and chariots were previously used in China, the <a href="/wiki/Wheelbarrow" title="Wheelbarrow">wheelbarrow</a> was first used in Han China in the 1st century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[306]</a></sup> Han artwork of horse-drawn chariots shows that the Warring-States-Era heavy wooden yoke placed around a horse\'s chest was replaced by the softer <i>breast strap</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c308&#8211;312,_319&#8211;323_307-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c308&#8211;312,_319&#8211;323-307">[307]</a></sup> Later, during the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Wei" title="Northern Wei">Northern Wei</a> (386&#8211;534), the fully developed <a href="/wiki/Horse_collar" title="Horse collar">horse collar</a> was invented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c308&#8211;312,_319&#8211;323_307-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham1986c308&#8211;312,_319&#8211;323-307">[307]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medicine">Medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicine" title="Traditional Chinese medicine">Traditional Chinese medicine</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Qigong_taiji_meditation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="551" data-file-width="758" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Qigong_taiji_meditation.jpg/220px-Qigong_taiji_meditation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Qigong_taiji_meditation.jpg/330px-Qigong_taiji_meditation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Qigong_taiji_meditation.jpg/440px-Qigong_taiji_meditation.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Qigong_taiji_meditation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The physical exercise chart; a painting on silk depicting the practice of <a href="/wiki/Qigong" title="Qigong">Qigong</a> Taiji; unearthed in 1973 in <a href="/wiki/Hunan" title="Hunan">Hunan</a> Province, China, from the 2nd-century BC <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Han" title="Western Han">Western Han</a> burial site of <a href="/wiki/Mawangdui" title="Mawangdui">Mawangdui</a>, Tomb Number 3.</div></div></div>\n<p>Han-era medical physicians believed that the human body was subject to the same forces of nature that governed the greater universe, namely the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmological" title="Cosmological">cosmological</a> cycles of yin and yang and the <a href="/wiki/Wu_Xing" title="Wu Xing">five phases</a>. Each <a href="/wiki/Zang-fu" title="Zang-fu">organ of the body</a> was associated with a particular phase. Illness was viewed as a sign that <i><a href="/wiki/Qi" title="Qi">qi</a></i> or "vital energy" channels leading to a certain organ had been disrupted. Thus, Han-era physicians prescribed medicine that was believed to counteract this imbalance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[308]</a></sup> For example, since the wood phase was believed to promote the fire phase, medicinal ingredients associated with the wood phase could be used to heal an organ associated with the fire phase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECsikszentmihalyi2006181&#8211;182_309-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECsikszentmihalyi2006181&#8211;182-309">[309]</a></sup> Besides dieting, Han physicians also prescribed <a href="/wiki/Moxibustion" title="Moxibustion">moxibustion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acupuncture" title="Acupuncture">acupuncture</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Calisthenics" title="Calisthenics">calisthenics</a> as methods of maintaining one\'s health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-310"><a href="#cite_note-310">[310]</a></sup> When <a href="/wiki/Surgery" title="Surgery">surgery</a> was performed by the Chinese physician <a href="/wiki/Hua_Tuo" title="Hua Tuo">Hua Tuo</a> (d. AD 208), he used <a href="/wiki/Anesthesia" title="Anesthesia">anesthesia</a> to numb his patients\' pain and prescribed a rubbing ointment that allegedly sped the process of healing surgical wounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Crespigny2007332_311-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Crespigny2007332-311">[311]</a></sup> Whereas the physician <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Zhongjing" title="Zhang Zhongjing">Zhang Zhongjing</a> (c. AD 150&#8211;c. 219) is known to have written the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shanghan_lun" title="Shanghan lun">Shanghan lun</a></i> ("Dissertation on Typhoid Fever"), it is thought that both he and Hua Tuo collaborated in compiling the <i><a href="/wiki/Shennong_Ben_Cao_Jing" title="Shennong Ben Cao Jing">Shennong Ben Cao Jing</a></i> medical text.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEde_Crespigny20071055_312-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEde_Crespigny20071055-312">[312]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Han_dynasty&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em;">\n<ul><li><a Zhuo</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Comparative_studies_of_the_Roman_and_Han_empires" title="Comparative studies of the Roman and Han empires">Comparative studies of the Roman and Han empires</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Imperial_China" title="Early Imperial China">Early Imperial China</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Mawangdui" relations</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Ten_Attendants" title="Ten Attendants">Ten Attendants</a></li></ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span
